+0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/feature1_herb.jpg","linkMd5":"8eea563cf83e684098112e506d1dfd8f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn39@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-15-922_3731cbaf514654ce.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":26475,"destBytes":18668,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/feature1_herb.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/herb_body1.jpg?itok=8nLodFks":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn24@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-17-112_57e43e5955377133.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/her_body2_0.jpg?itok=uVRce5Z8":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn32@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-19-798_8fb4aa3d281ceb8b.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/herb_body3.jpg?itok=75ZGnUdR":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn6@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-18-495_5b22cea500fafe5b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/U4BTdb9uAak":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn63@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-21-307_fe6042e3bc850e8b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696878},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Jared Della Valle’s eco family retreat is in tune with the landscape","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/8hjDECATdBE/cold-spring-retreat-jared-della-valle-alloy-usa","description":"<p>When architect and developer Jared Della Valle decided to embark on a project for his family and himself – a country home in the green expanses of New York State – his first instinct was to work with an existing rural structure. But after visiting a few sites and convening with his wife and children, it became apparent that a tailor made house that is both contemporary and respects its context and doesn’t burden the environment was the right way to go for them. The result was Cold Spring Residence, a clean, minimalist structure nestled into a slope overlooking the Hudson River Valley.  </p>\n<p>‘I started with certain preconceived notions of what I wanted. A fairly minimal, barn-like structure,’ recalls Della Valle. ‘After a point I kicked out my preconceptions and the land took the lead. Now, when you show up to the house you have no idea how big it is, you just see a black box.’ One of the site’s challenges and key zoning conditions was that any new structure built there should be as invisible as possible and not obstruct any views - essentially, like it isn’t even there. </p>\n<p>This felt appropriate and fully in line with the family’s goals, who had specified the need for a structure that touches ‘the land as lightly as possible’ and is environmentally friendly. Della Valle worked with passive house sustainability standards to create his retreat, including solar panels for year-round energy, a well-insulated building envelope and careful management of the site’s water resources. </p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287564\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Della Valle retreat outside \" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/2c6a3478.jpeg?itok=YqMjNKDH\" width=\"1200\" height=\"835\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The building, a two-volume structure with a black upper part and a sunken, exposed concrete lower half, may seem simple and fairly austere, but this was the result of an overall approach rather than simply an aesthetic choice for the family. ‘It’s the first time I had a completely empty canvas,’ explains Della Valle. ‘There is a degree of modesty, we didn’t want anybody to know it was here. By leaving the concrete and the wood being the only materials, we prioritised the landscape and nature.’ And while the building is in fact fairly generous, it does a great job at hiding its size within the slope remaining very low key and discreet – virtually invisible – from outside the property lines.</p>\n<p>This is an approach that resonates with Della Valle’s wider portfolio and ethos - his firm, Dumbo-based Alloy, is behind projects that offer a pared down, thoughtful simplicity, be it sensitive historical conversions or clean, geometric contemporary forms, both iterations often in the residential realm. ‘One design thesis of the house was that it had to be quite simple,’ he says. ‘I perceive my job as finding beauty in efficiency. We are trying to prioritise where the value is.’</p>\n<p>His family certainly helped with decision-making where needed, in what was, overall, an organic and peaceful process. ‘They were hands off but also completely there when I needed them – and set the primary goals for the project,’ says the architect. Now, the house caters for different needs, from relaxation and leisure activities through its generous living spaces, breathtaking views and outdoors areas and swimming pool, to casual workspaces – an office for Della Valle and a studio for his wife, an artist, both of which were used extensively during the recent pandemic-induced restrictions of movement in the State. </p>\n<p>As to whether the fact of being an architect as well as a developer made this process any easier for him, Della Valle explains that yes, it probably has: ‘I treated [the house project] a bit like my projects in the office. Once I completed my drawings, I didn’t want to make any changes. All of our time is very valuable, so once my family and I decided on what we wanted, I was very confident with those decisions - and I made a point of trying to decide everything in advance.’ </p>\n<p>Careful planning, a series of sustainability-led decisions, minimal decor and a collaborative approach between architect and clients led to a homely getaway that celebrates its site and is in tune with the context around it. §</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/8hjDECATdBE\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 Aug 2020 10:14:17 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2c6a3478.jpeg","linkMd5":"e61d38d5f4c4ff9ea499b43bc5d68d5c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn51@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-14-764_87544ae60bf2b974.webp","destWidth":812,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":123245,"destBytes":119112,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2c6a3478.jpeg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/2c6a3478.jpeg?itok=YqMjNKDH":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn4@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-21-122_dd98acb9842c72f7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/8hjDECATdBE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn65@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-19-286_c5345465ca792f43.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696878},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"MoreySmith’s central London office champions a sense of belonging","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/JaUyZ2YDGiI/moreysmith-office-interior-central-london","description":"<p>The swift decline and practical obsolescence of the traditional office has been remarked on by many, not least the millions of us who are now adjusting to very different patterns of life and work. But what of those whose job it is to shape those very spaces? One architecture and interior studio that’s had to face this particular reckoning rather sooner than others is MoreySmith, a London-based firm with copious experience and awards to its name and clients that include Sony, LVMH, JP Morgan and Coke. This summer, the studio moved into new, self-designed offices in central London, set across three floors of a renovated Victorian warehouse, close at hand to the company’s other workspace, which is retained as a creative studio.</p> \n<p>Linda Morey-Burrows is adamant that although density is down, some form of human connection is essential. ‘I do think that social space will be increasingly important,’ she says. The MoreySmith offices include plenty of flexible, collaborative spaces, a place to eat together and a Yoga space. While expansion was necessary due to the success of the business, so too was the need to improve social distancing. To that end, no staff sit directly facing each other, with 2m of clear space between each workstation.</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287509\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"moreysmith office desks\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/000052580001.jpg?itok=QwpxmIso\" width=\"1460\" height=\"2045\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>‘People will want their own persona space but they also want to be treated well,’ she says of the industry in general, adding that ‘the desk could even die [completely] with new technology.’ Instead, MoreySmith imagine offices becoming more like hybrid spaces, akin to a library or university study area rather than a rigorous call-centre rows of desks or cubicles.</p> \n<p>Their new workplace allows the studio to practice what it preaches, with plenty of greenery, sunlight, and a natural but pared back palette of materials (it is after all a place for experimenting with different finishes and fixtures). Artwork is also given price of place, with ‘5th March 1972’, a 7.5m long piece by Bob and Roberta Smith, serving as a focal point, with its textured surfaces of recycled wood.</p> \n<p>Sustainability is just as important as the quality of the space itself and Morey-Burrows and her team have taken care to re-use and re-purpose existing furniture and fittings wherever possible, including ex-display <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/lighting\" title=\"Lighting\">lighting</a> systems and a reclaimed timber floor.</p> \n<p>With their own space encouraging a more ascetic and focused attitude towards coming into the office, MoreySmith is also decoding a new future for their clients. ‘I’ve been looking at what materials are better for reducing virus transmission, like wood, untreated leather, copper,’ she says, adding that more research is needed into anti-viral design.</p> \n<p>‘I think that bigger brands and companies will have to work even harder to make their workspaces more attractive,’ she says, ‘it’s a bit like the struggle facing retail [before COVID]; those that did well created a more experiential shopping experience. The office is the same. Belonging and community are needed to draw people in.’ As for themselves, MoreySmith are proud to have created an office that ‘everyone was excited to come back to.’ §</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/JaUyZ2YDGiI\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 24 Aug 2020 10:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/000052580009.jpg","linkMd5":"f67dcde51719565d833750dbe3b60276","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn88@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-14-713_6b5e5e97a33890fd.webp","destWidth":372,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":26473,"destBytes":17802,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/000052580009.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/000052580001.jpg?itok=QwpxmIso":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn50@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-19-522_319f82fd1d45bd17.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/JaUyZ2YDGiI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn92@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-16-147_99c35cef7e972088.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696876},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"The sustainable collection made using clothing from Ghanian markets","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/R-cz3mc9_R0/collina-strada-browns-upcycled-collection","description":"<p>When it comes to pushing the boundaries of sustainable fashion, few brands have been as committed to the cause as Collina Strada – one of the three ascendant labels in New York, which we have profiled in our September ‘Style Special’ issue, which hits global newsstands this week. Originally founded by designer Hillary Taymour as a line of handbags and accessories back in 2009, today’s Collina Strada has become an experimental platform for highlighting social issues and creating awareness in exciting wearable form.<br />\n <br />\nThis week, Collina Strada unveils an exclusive capsule collection, developed for Browns in London, which builds upon the ideas Taymour explored in her Autumn/Winter 2020 collection. Made by repurposing discarded secondhand clothing purchased at Ghana’s Kantamanto market in Accra that originated from the United States, both collections are a critique of the waste and pollution that comes with the used goods economy.</p>\n<p><img alt=\"Collina Strada x Browns dress\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/collina5.jpg\" /></p>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\nCollina Strada x Browns\n</div>\n<p>Access to the fabrics was made possible through Taymour’s ongoing partnership with The OR Foundation, a US-based non-profit organisation disrupting the dominant consumer relationship with fashion. Dedicated to researching and exposing the ecological impacts of the fashion system, the foundation’s research project ‘Dead White Man’s Clothes’ – a literal translation of the Akan phrase for ‘second hand clothing’ – shines a light on the fact that over 15 million items of clothing from North America passes through Kantamanto every week for recycling, but around 40% leaves the market as waste, ending up in Ghana’s landfills, informal burn piles or even cast out to sea.<br />\n <br />\n‘I wanted to help bring awareness to the OR foundation and design pieces that can be sold online completely out of second hand waste,’ explains Taymour of how the collaboration came to be. ‘Every piece in this collection is made from t-shirts purchased in Ghana, [which] made it to the Katamanto markets that would eventually end up in a landfill. I went through hundreds of tees and individually laid out where each one would go on each garment with the pattern.’<br />\n <br />\nEach pieces in the capsule collection is conceived with an ease of wear. ‘[They’re] easy comfortable pieces that can go from day to night,’ she asserts. ‘These were all hand dyed and painted for the collection using interchangeable tie dyes [that] I have used throughout normal Collina Strada collections. Each garment is sewn locally and garment dyed in Bushwick.’<br />\n <br />\nThe garments are individually treated with an organic grid motif, which Taymour calls ‘Pool’. ‘I really am drawn to its fluid feeling as a dye,’ she shares. ‘It still feels like a tie-dye, without it necessarily screaming The Grateful Dead.’<br />\n <br />\nHolly Tenser, Browns’ womenswear buying manager, adds, ‘Hillary is one of the most relevant designers when it comes to conscious fashion. Her complete respect for the environment and the world we live in has been an integral part of her brand and ethos. She works with leftover fabrics to make her clothes and manages to draw prints or mix colours that are so powerful and unique, creating clothes that are instantly identifiable which is such an important attribute.’<br />\n <br />\nTo fully close the circle, Collina Strada will be donating a percentage of the wholesale cost – an amount that Browns has pledged to also match - to The OR Foundation. §<br />\n  </p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/R-cz3mc9_R0\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 24 Aug 2020 09:00:01 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/collinaf.jpg","linkMd5":"5243f1b34e2d81d8af2ac8e8a0faf08b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn96@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-15-598_5ab4f8957ae63e80.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":49693,"destBytes":44654,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/collinaf.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/collina5.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn83@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-17-871_2972ecf46081ced8.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/R-cz3mc9_R0":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn93@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-18-193_25d78b4e7729489d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696876},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Singapore Archifest pavilion ‘reclaims connectivity’ in an age of distance","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/MpEtzdxUlRk/reclaiming-connectivity-archifest-2020-pavilion-singapore","description":"<p>Singapore studio ADDP Architects and Los Angeles based OWIU Design have revealed their joint winning competition entry for a pavilion, which will be the centrepiece of this year’s Archifest event in Singapore this September. The two firms scooped the first prize to create a temporary flagship structure for the show, which is organised annually by the Singapore Institute of Architects; only this year, unsurprisingly, due to current events surrounding the pandemic, the Southeast Asian country’s celebration of architecture and the built environment will take place in the digital realm. </p>\n<p>Responding to this, the winning team’s concept drew on the ongoing global challenges and moulded its design to work with the format of virtual tours, ensuring the festival can still be accessible and present as ‘a platform to discuss, debate and dream about the possibilities surrounding architecture and the city of Singapore,’ explain the organisers. </p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287522\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Reclaiming Connectivity Archifest Pavilion design \" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rn3.jpg?itok=kksciP_u\" width=\"1460\" height=\"848\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>‘The pavilion will test new architectural ideas for post-Covid-19 design from its total prefab construction to its spatial layout, sustainable wooden material composition, functional tectonic glass design and light manipulation,’ say the designers. </p>\n<p>Entitled <em>Reclaiming Connectivity</em>, the structure features as its starting point OWIU Design Studio founding partner Amanda Gunawan’s photographic series on abstraction in the natural landscape. Blending these photographic prints on walls made of Light Emitting Surfaces glass, with spatial reflections on humanity’s innate need for connection, the design explores this yearning for togetherness that becomes even more prevalent in the current framework of international guidelines for social distancing. The designers propose creating private areas within public space as an alternative to isolation, illustrating their concept through timber, prefabricated arrangements within an open plan pavilion that offers glimpses of natural scenes. </p>\n<p>The pavilion, which is designed to be physically built within 48 hours using prefabrication methods and 99 per cent sustainable FSC-certified timber, is still planned to appear in its physical form next year in the Singapore City gallery; but for now, it will be available to visit virtually from 25 September 2020 through the Archifest website. §</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/MpEtzdxUlRk\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 18 Aug 2020 09:07:25 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/rn3.jpg","linkMd5":"31ceb4fbee59a762dcf1d2318d1d4ef9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn4@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-15-927_140e7d7a66d97f5d.webp","destWidth":922,"destHeight":535,"sourceBytes":116961,"destBytes":114690,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/rn3.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rn3.jpg?itok=kksciP_u":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn59@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-20-089_18830681642a7200.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/MpEtzdxUlRk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn61@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-19-312_1af771da5ac76257.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696878},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"A new museum near Shanghai celebrates Chinese cultural history","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/71Zqp8D1BWo/zhang-yan-cultural-museum-horizontal-design","description":"<p>In the suburbs of Shanghai, in Chonggu Town, a new museum celebrates the birthplace of Shanghai’s ancient culture and the history of the Fuquan Mountain culture. Working within the remains of historic architecture, Shenzhen based architecture studio Horizontal Design carefully balanced renovation with new building design to create a voluminous collection of concrete forms that organically grows from the existing historic architecture.</p>\n<p>Recently, efforts have been made to restore and revive the Zhang Yan Village, which dates back to the Tang and Song dynasties and appears in Chinese literature and cultural history. Across the village, new design projects are taking place to embrace its past, while updating it for contemporary uses too, so old and new can co-exist.</p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287355\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Courtyard\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/37_part_of_the_water_yard_0.jpg?itok=62WCk-kx\" width=\"1460\" height=\"2190\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>To start their design process for the museum, the architects researched and analysed the existing site. Several structures – the Village History Hall and the dilapidated Zhang Family’s House – formed the starting point for the new design, and closely neighbouring buildings such as a local temple, defined the spatial and aesthetic qualities of the design too.</p>\n<p>On the site was a former family home dating back to the Qing dynasty. While it was in a state of disrepair, the architects chose to preserve what was left, and built a respectful addition to create one of the exhibition halls on the same footprint. The new architecture was slightly offset, so it appears to float within the ruin. The design follows the traditional atrium typology with inward sloping roofs, echoing the life of the former structure.</p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287356\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Exhibition hall\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/18_the_interior_view_from_the_south_to_the_north_of_exhibition_1_0.jpg?itok=UFHNwpM_\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1322\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287359\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Exhibition hall and wall\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/21_exhibition_1_and_the_ancient_wall_0.jpg?itok=GtwfLRwB\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1174\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The better preserved Village History Hall was given a full renovation – the wooden load-bearing structure was repaired and revealed and partition walls were removed, so the original atrium space can again be celebrated. An anodized aluminium flooring, chosen to resist humidity, was laid throughout the exhibition halls, so there is consistency throughout the museum.</p>\n<p>Further research revealed another historic building to the north, so a third exhibition hall was built on this footprint. Anodized aluminium was used for the flooring and ceiling, so this space feels more contemporary than the first two spaces.</p>\n<p>Between the new volumes, bamboo plants and a water feature create a calming oasis for the community to enjoy. A grey brick masonry wall encloses the space forming a sheltered courtyard. §</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/71Zqp8D1BWo\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 14 Aug 2020 13:50:11 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/37_part_of_the_water_yard.jpg","linkMd5":"ade1bf1d9e8813a74a1b1b62687add92","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn32@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-15-430_f6e8dd725289f270.webp","destWidth":377,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":36271,"destBytes":33840,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/37_part_of_the_water_yard.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/37_part_of_the_water_yard_0.jpg?itok=62WCk-kx":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn85@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-19-632_f55588f782b2f63d.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/18_the_interior_view_from_the_south_to_the_north_of_exhibition_1_0.jpg?itok=UFHNwpM_":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn91@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-19-269_fc1e20e9fbb1952a.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/21_exhibition_1_and_the_ancient_wall_0.jpg?itok=GtwfLRwB":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn36@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-19-188_53fe0dd62b9bac43.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/71Zqp8D1BWo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn100@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-16-257_202ebf68bcfb1aef.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696879},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Outer space: Rogan Gregory thinks big in his new LA studio ","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/srsCxvGzFRg/rogan-gregory-new-LA-studio","description":"<p>The exodus of New Yorkers from the Big Apple for admittedly practical reasons, like having more space or striking a better work-life balance, has perennially ebbed and flowed; it seems on the upswing now with large corporate offices closed until 2021 and working from home becoming the norm. For the sculptor and longtime New Yorker Rogan Gregory however, his recent move to Los Angeles and taking up a new studio space in Santa Monica was independent of the pandemic.</p> \n<p>‘I do a lot of work out in Los Angeles already, I have my foundries out there,’ shares Gregory, who lived in New York City for over 20 years before moving to Montauk and then to Amagansett in Long Island, where he’s lived full-time for the last five. ‘I’m very sensitive to the natural world and while I find and see inspiration in the change of seasons, I‘m more inspired by life; things growing and life-forms. In LA, anything goes; whatever you want to grow can grow, as long as you’ve got some water. There’s a lot of [other] reasons, like weather, but really in the end, I literally needed a change of scenery.’&nbsp;&nbsp;</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287238\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Rogan Gregory in his studio in Santa Monica next to a sculpture\" title=\"Portrait of Rogan Gregory \" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rogangregorystudio.jpg?itok=5L5cGWW2\" width=\"944\" height=\"1180\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Rogan Gregory stands next to his bronze \n <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/sculpture\" title=\"Sculpture\">sculpture</a>, \n <em>Rupestris</em> in his LA studio \n</div> \n<p>In his new studio, which was originally a depot for garbage trucks with a dirt floor that was then later paved over, Gregory is able to fully realise the large-scale sculptures that he has been most recently focusing on. The minimal, warehouse-style space perfectly serves as a blank canvas for Gregory to sketch, mould and build his wide array of works, which range from statuesque, yet amorphous chairs, and sea creature-like pendant lights, to sculptural bronze floor-standing lights that resemble fauna. ‘It’s a very simple white box. I put a skylight in and a roll-down door, and that’s exactly what I need,’ he states.</p> \n<p>‘In LA, I have a lot more space, there’s less of a grind, I can think clearer. From an environmental standpoint, everything is more open and bigger in California. In Amagansett, there are a lot of trees, so your point of view is blocked. I love trees, don’t get me wrong, and I will miss them, but generally speaking, when everything is bigger, you tend to go bigger,’ he says, sharing that he’s currently working on a few outdoor commissions. ‘Obviously when you’re outdoors, the sky’s the limit.’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287239\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Sculptures and reference objects on shelves in Rogan Gregory’s new studio in LA\" title=\"Artist Rogan Gregory’s new LA studio\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rogangregorystudio_mr_skj_05.jpg?itok=9NZDp86T\" width=\"944\" height=\"1180\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Maquettes for commissions and an animal skull arranged on the shelves in Gregory’s studio&nbsp; \n</div> \n<p>‘Much of Rogan’s work is commission-based,’ says Evan Snyderman, principal of R &amp; Company, the gallery that represents Gregory. ‘What is unique about his approach is how much he cares about the way the work connects to the environment it’s created for and how it complements the architecture or the interior it exists within. Every single piece is made in his studio by his own hands, so there is a direct connection to the work, which in turn creates an energy. Clients are drawn to the gesture of a line or a form that alludes to the works’ sensuality.’</p> \n<p>Whether it’s the congregation of arching bronze lights that look ‘almost like aliens gathering before they get released’ or a striking black-and-white <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/sculpture\" title=\"Sculpture\">sculpture</a> that Gregory refers to as ‘the horse’, which you can sit on and will eventually be realised in bronze, all of the work starts off as either a drawing or maquette.</p> \n<blockquote>\n <p> ‘I get up in the morning and draw, or I make maquettes. If I do that for two to three hours, I feel fine. It’s almost like therapy. If I don’t do it, then I’m grouchy’ </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>‘I get up in the morning and draw, or I make maquettes. If I do that for two to three hours, I feel fine. It’s almost like therapy. If I don’t do it, then I’m grouchy,’ Gregory says. ‘Anything you can think of, you can realise the general idea of [in a sketch]. Making a maquette is a much more accurate depiction of what the eventual piece will be. It depends on the piece really.’</p> \n<p>With much of his inspiration drawn from the natural world, albeit loosely, like ‘thinking about what life could exist on an exoplanet or some liveable planet elsewhere’, or through a lens of weightlessness ‘like you’d find in the ocean’, there is a simultaneously organic and futuristic energy to Gregory’s pieces. The artist, who likes to surf and dive, says, ‘I imagine that a lot of animals that exist in the deep, deep ocean might be similar to what could exist in a place with a different atmosphere, different gravitational pull and the absence of light.’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287240\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rogangregorystudio_mr_skj_06_bw.jpg?itok=TWwBQaGa\" width=\"944\" height=\"1180\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Gregory’s\n <em> Asymmetrical</em> chair (left) and \n <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/sculpture\" title=\"Sculpture\">sculpture</a> \n <em>Lazy Rider </em>(right) \n</div> \n<p>Despite producing more sculptural pieces of late, Gregory asserts that he does not have a preference for making non-functional over functional pieces. ‘I vacillate between the two. I don’t feel like I want to restrain myself. It wasn’t okay 10 to 15 years ago for people to do both the functional and the fine art, but nowadays [you can],’ he says. ‘I was a clothing designer for a long time, and you had to be functional. So I do come from the belief that things have to be well made. I just like things to be well made.’ §</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/srsCxvGzFRg\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" The devil is certainly in the detail for a new project from her studio – the interior makeover of an apartment in Rome.</p> \n<p>‘Everything must be in the right place and we should not be afraid to express bold concepts, even in a domestic setting,’ Venturoni continues. The domestic site here is 80 sq m, and is filled with warm tones of red, earth and beige that are an ode to the traditional city it is housed within.</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287337\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Clothes in the room\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/02_living4_studioventuroni_phbonichi_trevi.jpg?itok=bv7PMPbb\" width=\"1460\" height=\"895\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287338\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Kitchen area\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/06_kitchen-2_studioventuroni_phbonechi_trevi.jpg?itok=F0Z536tO\" width=\"629\" height=\"944\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>Venturoni stripped back the original set up of thick masonry walls and custom-made wooden cabinets to allow light to flood through the large windows and doorways. Included in the product mix are Driade armchairs, <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/kartell\" title=\"Kartell\">Kartell</a> lamps, a Magis chair and Flos lamp.</p> \n<p>The joined living and kitchen area is also where the studio takes some darker turns with the doors and kitchen units, bringing modernity to the heritage building that is a stone’s throw away from the Trevi fountain by architect Nicola Salvi and Giuseppe Pannini. In contrast, the gold detailing, and the mustard and blue soft furnishings in the bedroom create a more classic atmosphere that nod to the historic surroundings.</p> \n<p>Venturoni has studied and worked in from cities Phoenix to Copenhagen, and these influences can be seen in the apartment from the minimal sconces and table lamps to brass accents. While she is global in her inspiration, her heart remains in Milan and its innate culture of design. §</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/BgEmRve2lao\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 13 Aug 2020 10:46:46 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/01_entrance_dining1_studioventuroni_phbonechi_trevi_rev.jpg","linkMd5":"19cdec868e916d2e3c4e2395f8872906","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn24@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-15-303_9753741af821cbfb.webp","destWidth":922,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":71682,"destBytes":57640,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/01_entrance_dining1_studioventuroni_phbonechi_trevi_rev.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/02_living4_studioventuroni_phbonichi_trevi.jpg?itok=bv7PMPbb":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn45@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-18-987_95c4d74bdefe8b3d.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/06_kitchen-2_studioventuroni_phbonechi_trevi.jpg?itok=F0Z536tO":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn31@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-16-645_207c2e241f593912.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/BgEmRve2lao":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn78@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-21-388_a1653f49b394f811.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696879},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Suburban Silicon Valley home provides a refuge for nature","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/gW6uzHpfGPE/dawnridge-house-field-architecture-california-usa","description":"<p>For Palo Alto based Field Architects, composing a new build home on a plot in one of Silicon Valley’s suburbs was all about connecting with the land; getting to know their site and making, not only the most its surface potential, but also ensuring the landscape has room to breathe and existing natural conditions are respected and incorporated into their proposal. The result is Dawnbridge, a private house created next to a creek and sheltered underneath large oak trees. </p>\n<p>‘Dawnridge re-envisions the site as a reawakened fragment of the natural environment settled in a developed setting,’ say the architects. Studying their parcel’s topography and native flora and fauna, the team identified their focus: to attempt to ‘revive a fragment of the area’s natural environment’, in what is an increasingly urban neighbourhood. </p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287175\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"FIELD architecture Dawnridge facade\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/field_dawnridge_37.jpg?itok=8pYNrnEC\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1094\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>‘We designated more than half of the site as a natural, protected habitat, prioritising the restoration of an intermittent freshwater stream that winds along the northern edge of the site,’ continue the architects. The remainder of the plot – the chosen buildable area – is now occupied by a fairly low volume, softened by vertical timber cladding that guides the gaze upwards towards the trees and sky. Rounded corners and expanses of glass that create reflections and a dialogue with the garden reinforce the structure’s subtlety. </p>\n<p>The internal programme allows for generous spaces and comfortable living, but works in tandem with the landscape. The house’s only dining area, for example, is in fact located outdoors, blurring the boundaries between inside and out. Materials and colours (ash wood, light grey concrete) were selected to match the environment – built and natural. Tall ceilings, large opening and terraces make sure residents are continually connected with the garden. </p>\n<p>This residence was designed as a refuge – both for the family that lives in it, but also the area’s delicate ecosystem, championing an architectural approach that promotes health and wellbeing for all. §</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/gW6uzHpfGPE\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 13 Aug 2020 11:20:37 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/field_dawnridge_48.jpg","linkMd5":"e381dc337a76dbc2f20aa65d788c2bf1","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn36@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-14-783_0532dd2986ca92a3.webp","destWidth":424,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":46784,"destBytes":43820,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/field_dawnridge_48.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/field_dawnridge_37.jpg?itok=8pYNrnEC":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn100@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-17-010_fe5368e046e44005.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/gW6uzHpfGPE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn4@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-16-889_75f5ee9ad5e958e1.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696879},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Remembering legendary Brazilian modernist Jorge Zalszupin (1922-2020)","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/aI0hD7GsOJA/in-memoriam-jorge-zalszupin-obituary-1922-2020","description":"<p>The design world has lost a master with the passing of Jorge Zalszupin, aged 98 years. Polish by birth and Brazilian at heart, he lived life to the fullest, often describing his story as ‘a succession of miracles’ – a personal reflection no doubt born of the same innate elegance as his iconic furniture.</p> \n<p>He was born Jerzy Zalszupin on 1 June 1922 in Warsaw, Poland to Jewish parents. At the age of 18 he moved to Bucharest to escape Nazi occupation, graduating from Romania’s top architecture program in 1944. He began his career as an architect in France, rebuilding after WWII. Here, the lasting weight of war and unrelenting reverence for history and its traditions took a toll on this modernist eager to move forward.In 1949, he emigrated to Brazil, excited by the possibilities in a country that was rapidly modernising and becoming more politically and culturally progressive at equal pace. He was also deeply inspired by the innovative work of Oscar Niemeyer and other modernists of the time.</p> \n<p>Within a decade of arriving awestruck in Rio de Janeiro with no prospects or Portuguese language skills, the tenacious Zalszupin had made a name for himself, started a family, became a Brazilian citizen and launched his own architecture firm, Escritório Téchnico Prumo in São Paulo. He also started collaborating with Niemeyer himself, designing furniture for the most important commission in the country’s history, the sweeping new capital city of Brasília.</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287579\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Espasso trolly by zalszupin\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/zalszupin_trolley_2.jpg?itok=lF5WHYb5\" width=\"920\" height=\"564\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"mt-3 image-caption\">\n  Tea cart by Jorge Zalszupin, 1947. \n <em>Courtesy Galerie Chastel-Maréchal. © Agence Phar - J. Beylard et V. Luc</em> \n</div> \n<p>Zalszupin began designing furniture at the requests of his clients, who wanted continuity between the interiors and the modern homes and office spaces he designed for them. With a serious dearth of options in the market and a ban on imported goods, he began designing furniture and coordinating fabrication with a few skilled craftsmen. It wasn’t long until he founded L’Atelier, a design studio, manufacturer and retailer of high-quality modern furniture.</p> \n<p>The Poltrona Dinamarquesa, or Danish Chair was his first independent effort, and is considered an icon of <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/brazilian-architecture-and-design\" title=\"Brazilian design\" class=\"word-link\">Brazilian design</a> – even with an overt nod to Danish masters Hans Wegner and Finn Juhl. The sensual curves of the arms and slim, tapered legs give it Brazilian flair, recalling certain architectural details of Niemeyer’s Alvorada Palace in Brasília. This is the point where Zalszupin’s legacy begins.</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287580\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Brazilian design sofa\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/06-espasso-brazilian-design.jpg?itok=4JArhS2S\" width=\"770\" height=\"472\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"mt-3 image-caption\">\n  ’Brasiliana’ sofa, by Jorge Zalszupin \n</div> \n<p>‘He is a legend of Brazilian <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/modernism\" title=\"Modernism\">modernism</a>, who created timeless furniture designs of exceptional quality,’ said Lissa Carmona Tozzi, CEO of ETEL, who together with her mother, designer Etel Carmona worked directly with Jorge to reissue a range of his pieces in 2006. They ensured the longevity of the works by adhering to restrictions on many of the rare Brazilian woods originally used in the products, such as jacarandá or Brazilian rosewood.</p> \n<p>Other iconic pieces, like the Poltronas Presidencial and Brasíliana, or the Pétalas table series pushed Brazilian furniture manufacturing forward in 1959, pioneering the use of bent-plywood furniture construction in the country. While he was a master at working with native wood species, he also explored other materials with the same eye for innovation, from steel to marble to fibreglass and even moulded plastics later in his career. These techniques are now commonplace in Brazil, but before Zalszupin helped develop a more sophisticated supply chain, they weren’t.</p> \n<p>This lesser-known legacy as an industrialist, innovator and leader in an era of protectionism is significant too. ‘I’m not sure I could be a furniture designer in Brazil at this level today without the contributions of Jorge Zalszupin as a manufacturer, innovator and a businessperson. He and his teams laid the foundation for our industry,’ said São Paulo-based furniture designer Guilherme Wentz.The succession of miracles that Jorge Zalszupin saw as his life will continue, as his legacy inspires and supports designers, enthusiasts and industry professionals for generations to come. §</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/aI0hD7GsOJA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 18 Aug 2020 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/jorgezalszupin.jpg","linkMd5":"a1b3baf61ee5d3e51495766c2f577185","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn11@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-15-929_82a404c5a891fe91.webp","destWidth":455,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":72016,"destBytes":70408,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/jorgezalszupin.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/zalszupin_trolley_2.jpg?itok=lF5WHYb5":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn99@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-20-577_341635f64c7d0841.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/06-espasso-brazilian-design.jpg?itok=4JArhS2S":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn77@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-20-856_28a2c6136369db5b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/aI0hD7GsOJA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn22@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-17-922_480507ad4741342c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696878},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Dyson family plan to open art collection to the public with new gallery ","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/eIM5UYxlfZs/dyson-family-modern-art-gallery-dodington-park","description":"<p>For many&nbsp;private art&nbsp;collections, private is the operative word. But for Sir James and&nbsp;Lady Deirdre Dyson, it’s time their impressive collection came up for air, and free public appreciation. The family has just announced&nbsp;plans to build&nbsp;a new pavilion gallery in the grounds of their home in the Cotswolds.</p> \n<p>The proposed gallery, which has just been submitted for planning approval, has been designed by Wilkinson Eyre architects and will be&nbsp;sited on the northern edge of the Dysons’ sprawling Dodington Park home&nbsp;in South Gloucestershire, a stone’s throw from the current Dyson HQ.</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287491\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/05-access-cgi.jpg?itok=J0DYNu8r\" width=\"1460\" height=\"727\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  The entrance of the Dysons’ planned gallery&nbsp;which will house their extensive modern art collection&nbsp; \n</div> \n<p>The estate’s&nbsp;palatial main house, built in the 18th century, is Grade I listed. Even the grounds –&nbsp;which span&nbsp;300 acres and were&nbsp;originally laid out by Capability Brown in 1764 – boast a Grade II listing, including the ancient brick wall which will encircle&nbsp;the proposed art space.</p> \n<p>The structure will be, in the words of architect Chis Wilkinson, ‘low key’: modern, discreet and in tune&nbsp;with&nbsp;its neighbouring architecture. It will comprise two floors (ground and basement) and feature angular exterior steel columns clad in bronze to echo&nbsp;the tone of the surrounding brick. The overhanging diagrid roof will be carpeted in sedum plants to minimise environmental intrusion. It will be free for the public to visit (albeit on limited days of the year), but pre-booking is essential.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p> \n<p>For industrial designer and inventor James Dyson, whose business success began with a bagless vacuum and resulted in a multi-billion pound empire, art appreciation is deep-rooted –&nbsp;he himself was&nbsp;once an aspiring painter. The same can be said for his&nbsp;wife, Deirdre Dyson, an accomplished artist and carpet designer in her own right. The pair met in 1966 while studying at London’s Byam Shaw School of Art (James later studied <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/furniture-design\" title=\"Furniture design\">furniture design</a> at the Royal College of Art)&nbsp;and have since amassed a&nbsp;star-studded&nbsp;array of <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/painting\" title=\"Painting\">painting</a>&nbsp;and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/sculpture\" title=\"Sculpture\">sculpture</a>, with Pop Art as a recurring theme.&nbsp;</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287492\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Interior plan of new Dyson gallery in South Gloucestershire which will house works by David Hockney, Andy Warhol, Roy Lichtenstein and Pablo Picasso\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/07-interior-02-cgi.jpg?itok=zFhngggl\" width=\"1460\" height=\"727\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\"> \n <div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n  <div id=\"file-287493\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n   <div class=\"content\"> \n    <img alt=\"Inside the Dyson gallery space which will host works by David Hockney, Pablo Picasso, Roy Lichtenstein and Andy Warhol\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/06-interior-01-cgi.jpg?itok=Gi22eUiQ\" width=\"1460\" height=\"728\" />\n   </div> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p>Inside the proposed gallery space in the grounds of&nbsp;Dodington Park, which will house works by the likes of David Hockney, Roy Lichtenstein,&nbsp;Pablo Picasso and Andy Warhol </p>\n</div> \n<p>Among the works are Roy Lichtenstein’s <em>Crying Girl</em> (1963), a late Andy Warhol print from his <em>Toy Series </em>(1983), Peter Blake’s <em>Twins in the Tea Garden</em> (1999) and David Hockney’s&nbsp;<em>Domestic Interior Scene, Broadchalke, Wiltshire</em>&nbsp;(1963). The roll call continues with&nbsp;Pablo Picasso, Yves Klein, Victor Vasarely,&nbsp;painted steel sculptures by&nbsp;Allen Jones and even more Hockneys.&nbsp;‘The most vibrant and significant period in British Pop Art came from the Royal College of Art in the first half of the Sixties and the collection is clearly informed by this,’&nbsp;explains Tim Marlow, chief executive and director of London’s Design Museum.&nbsp;‘It is wide-ranging and personal but also has an underlying coherence and quality.’</p> \n<p>Dodington Art Gallery, which is slated&nbsp;for completion in 2021, is a project reinforced by good intentions: sustainable construction, sensitivity to its surroundings and the promise of free public access to a collection of art Marlow describes as ‘compelling and important’.&nbsp;§</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287494\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img artists to produce face masks, with all proceeds going to artist support for those affected by Covid-19 or to international relief efforts. ‘Good design enhances and beautifies function. It sweetens the necessity of an object,’ says Amelie von Wedel, art advisor at Wedel Art and founder of Wedel Art Collective.</p>\n<h2>Barbara Kruger</h2>\n<p>Pictured top, the first artist Wedel has partnered with, Barbara Kruger, has rethought the bold colour and statements which define her artwork. <em>Sign Language</em> is consistent with the effects of political structures which Kruger explores in her work, here emphasising how vital the role speech – or silence – can play in shaping our society.</p>\n<h2>Jenny Holzer</h2>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287142\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"jenny holzer\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/jenny-mask.jpg?itok=fvTq0fcI\" width=\"1200\" height=\"1400\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\nFace mask by Jenny Holzer, courtesy of Wedel Art Collective. Masks will be available exclusively at Matches Fashion from 24 August. <em>Photography: Zuumeo</em>\n</div>\n<p>Succinctly cutting to the heart of the issue is Jenny Holzer, whose mask offers a useful reminder as to why they are necessary at all. <em>You - Me</em> is in the spirit of the powerful statements she makes through her artwork, with concise and thoughtful alternative commentaries.</p>\n<h2>Rosemarie Trockel</h2>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287320\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"face mask\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"6\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rosemarie-mask_0.jpg?itok=N5QRbTJ5\" width=\"1200\" height=\"1400\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\nFace mask by Rosemarie Trockel, courtesy of Wedel Art Collective. Masks will be available exclusively at Matches Fashion from 24 August. <em>Photography: Zuumeo</em>\n</div>\n<p>Rosemarie Trockel has created four different versions of her Wedel Art Collective mask, each paying tribute to four of the women she has featured in work in the past which explores issues around gender and identity. Hannah Arendt, Nina Simone, Marguerite Duras and Agnes Martin’s names are legible from only a safe distance of 1.5 - 2m. With no explanation, their names invite conversation.</p>\n<h2>Rashid Johnson</h2>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287143\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Rashid Johnson face mask\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rashid-mask.jpg?itok=nU9tR2tK\" width=\"1200\" height=\"1400\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\nFace mask by Rashid Johnson, courtesy of Wedel Art Collective. Masks will be available exclusively at Matches Fashion from 24 August. <em>Photography: Zuumeo</em>\n</div>\n<p>Rashid Johnson draws on his <em>Broken Men</em> artwork for his mask design. The works – uneven mosaics of anxiety – ask philosophical questions as to how we can endure and fight with dignity, issues which come to the fore in times of crisis.</p>\n<h2>Lorna Simpson</h2>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287144\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Lorna simpson face mask\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"4\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/lorna-mask.jpg?itok=n-nZ4qmB\" width=\"1200\" height=\"1400\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\nFace mask by Lorna Simpson, courtesy of Wedel Art Collective. It is part of a series of monochromatic paintings which she has created by layering the faces of women who appeared in adverts for <em>Ebony </em>magazine, a surreal take on the nature of gender and race.</p>\n<h2>Raymond Pettibon</h2>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287145\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"raymond pettibon face mask\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"5\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/raymond-mask.jpg?itok=spmEz7dW\" width=\"1200\" height=\"1400\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\nFace mask by Raymond Pettibon, courtesy of Wedel Art Collective. Masks will be available exclusively at Matches Fashion from 24 August. <em>Photography: Zuumeo</em>\n</div>\n<p>The final artist collaboration is with Raymond Pettibon, whose work in the past has paid reference to an eclectic mash of sources, with everything from pop and commercial culture to classic literary texts sources of inspiration. Dubai","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/45sYxINBK2U/secret-room-speakeasy-dubai","description":"<p>Dubai’s MO has long been to upsize every quotidian experience; whether to create the world’s biggest mall or an artificial island that’s visible from space.</p> \n<p>Little surprise then that even the ideal of the traditional speakeasy as a small cozy affair has now been turned on its head. For Toronto-based Studio Paolo Ferrari, the visual references splice Rome’s Villa Farnese, the final Louis XIV-esque bedroom scene in <em>2001: A Space Odyssey,</em> and Anish Kapoor’s <em>Cloud Gate</em> <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/sculpture\" title=\"Sculpture\">sculpture</a> in Chicago.</p> \n<p>The result is an unapologetically theatrical set-piece in which clear cast resin tables, 1960s furnishings, and smoked oak panels flank a bronze-hued fiberglass bar whose sinuous curves recall both Dali and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/zaha-hadid\" title=\"Zaha Hadid\">Zaha Hadid</a>. The bathrooms, lined with glass floors and mirrored walls, are attention grabbers – though, perhaps, not as much as the hidden doors in the main room that swing open for VIPs to literally drive into the speakeasy directly from the carpark. §</p> \n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/45sYxINBK2U\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 24 Aug 2020 08:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"4b417b5feb00019824254f562e232cdc","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/45sYxINBK2U":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn52@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-16-157_26685a39d055e65b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696876},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"University of Virginia memorial honours ‘lives, labour and perseverance’ of enslaved African Americans","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/JGDMhV7yoPw/memorial-of-enslaved-labor-howeler-yoon-university-of-virginia-usa","description":"<p>A new spatial memorial in the USA honours the ‘lives, labour and perseverance’ of the enslaved African Americans who built and maintained the University of Virginia (UVA) in Charlottesville. The Memorial to Enslaved Laborers has been unveiled to a design by Boston based architects Höweler + Yoon in collaboration with historian and designer Dr. Mabel O. Wilson (Studio&amp;), Gregg Bleam Landscape Architect, community facilitator Dr. Frank Dukes, and artist Eto Otitigbe. </p>\n<p>The piece, which sits on the green grounds of the campus, is dedicated to the some 4,000 enslaved persons, who worked on the Grounds of UVA between 1817 and 1865, either owned or rented by the University for this task. Acknowledging both the dehumanising violence involved in this, as well as the enslaved people’s hope, sense of community, resistance and resilience, the memorial ‘creates a vital public place to understand, learn, and remember their contribution to the University,’ explains the institution. </p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287629\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"UVA memorial Höweler + Yoon aerial\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/mel_ss_aerial_grounds_sanjaysuchak.jpg?itok=PIZ0F8do\" width=\"1460\" height=\"973\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n<em>Photography: Sanjay Suchak</em>\n</div>\n<p>The design includes a low, round structure, 80ft in diameter, which rises from the ground, almost mirroring the circular form of the University’s iconic Rotunda dome nearby. Made of local Virginia Mist granite, it features concentric rings that wrap around a planted circular meeting ground. A water table presents a timeline honoring the enslaved and a concave wall of memory marks the names of individuals in the community. There is also a textured convex sloping stone inscribed with a portrait of Isabella Gibbons, an enslaved domestic worker at UVA, who later became a teacher in Charlottesville – this piece is by Otitigbe.</p>\n<p>‘The Memorial is not just about memory,’ says Meejin Yoon, co-founder of Höweler + Yoon. ‘It is about the present, and the work that we all need to be engaged in today. The Memorial is about actively bringing people together. It is about opening up dialogue, between visitors and individuals, but also between the University and the community. It is rebuilding a relationship and rebuilding trust. These are the necessary steps in the contemporary conversations about race and repair.’ §</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/JGDMhV7yoPw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 20 Aug 2020 15:42:28 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020_ak128-ff_crop_alankarchmer.jpg","linkMd5":"5f5c488f1e516b1b6c90719f0027c3e0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn96@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-14-737_47c0be897dbf5ac1.webp","destWidth":848,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":143898,"destBytes":142194,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020_ak128-ff_crop_alankarchmer.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/mel_ss_aerial_grounds_sanjaysuchak.jpg?itok=PIZ0F8do":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn82@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-21-464_7b1fbc2a7fbdce8a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/JGDMhV7yoPw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn72@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-16-033_2ceb3530bfedcc3f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696878},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Paper goods: the brand championing sustainable stationery","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/KV42iEihNAw/maurele-champion-stylish-sustainable-stationery","description":"<p>For anyone looking to get reacquainted with the lost practice of letter writing, the Canadian paper goods company Maurèle brings with it all the class and elegance of a bygone era while accompanied by a distinct contemporary edge. Launched in November by the husband-and-wife duo, Nick D’Urbano and Cece de la Montagne, Maurèle is designed to be a source of refuge from the deluge of electronic correspondence, direct messaging and overall productivity, instead encouraging quiet and contemplation with its stylish suite of customisable and sustainably made <a href=\"https://www.wallpaper.com/tags/stationery\" target=\"_self\">stationery</a>.</p> \n<p>‘We’ve always been big believers of analogue, tactile experiences,’ says de la Montagne, an architect who’s also behind the accessory label <a href=\"https://www.wallpaper.com/fashion/atelier-yul-carryalls-are-the-perfect-creators-companion\">Atelier YUL</a>. ‘There is something beautiful, peaceful and intimate about writing, sketching, or reading a book. In this age of perennial distraction and digital overload, these rare moments have become even more important.’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286920\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/notecard_initials.jpg?itok=lVcYtt5o\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1824\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>‘What we felt was lacking in the market was a focus on the simple pleasures of reading and writing,’ adds D’Urbano, who’s currently building on his business experience by pursuing an advanced degree in literature. ‘There are a lot of heritage brands whose aesthetic is more classical and ornamental. There are also many trendy, playful brands. Particularly when it comes to personalized paper goods, we found few offerings with a contemporary, clean and timeless aesthetic. We hope to fill that gap with a brand and set of products that celebrate a subtle, understated design, beautiful <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/typography\" title=\"Typography\">typography</a> and a rich assortment of paper options.’</p> \n<p>Under their keen eye, the Montreal-based firm has selected paper stock made by a fourth generation family-run mill in the United States. Apart from its papers’ exquisite quality, the mill also uses steam plants, wind-powered energy and continually recycles water for reuse. Available in an array of textures and shades, the house collection of letter paper and note cards comes matched with its signature debossed envelopes in a winning shade of sage green.</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286921\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/set_catalonia.jpg?itok=ga7E-Frb\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1825\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>Better still, Maurèle invites customers to create their own personalized stationery from a series of templates inspired by the desks of some very famous creative figures. Whether it’s the oversized, bold centered type of the Catalonia, which mirrors the personal stationery of Salvador Dali (granted his moniker used to take up 1/3 of a page), or the minimalist, mirrored proportions of the Taliesin, a tribute to the personal letterheads of both Frank Lloyd Wright and Helmut Lang, each template can be further tailored to one’s tastes by selecting from Maurèle‘s library of typefaces, which have been curated from the world’s most foremost foundries.</p> \n<p>‘We became obsessed,’ de la Montagne recalls. ‘We spent months scouring the web, blogs and our design networks to come up with a list of type designers and type foundries from different corners of the globe. We wanted to curate an assortment that featured both big and smaller foundries as well as independent type designers not tied to a particular studio. We collaborated with a handful of really talented designers to grow our list of potential fonts to 60. We then spent countless hours debating and refining until we whittled that number down to 13. We studied their forms, rhythm, and printed hundreds of pages to analyze letter combinations.’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286922\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/love_a6_love.jpg?itok=-4V5O0wa\" width=\"1000\" height=\"1250\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>‘We also looked for fonts with a deep, rich narrative,’ D’Urbano says, while sharing that a template inspired by Ray Charles is set to come. ‘Each typeface was chosen not only for it’s immaculate design but also because it tells a story.’</p> \n<p>Aided by Maurèle’s easy-to-use online interface, and printed in Montreal on a Heidelberg press by third generation printers, and it has never been easier to create customised stationery that displays an eye for both quality and design. §</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/KV42iEihNAw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"
