and&nbsp;public cultural activities hosted at the MPavilion \n</div> \n<p>Essays on each pavilion by distinguished writers, including <a href=\"https://www.wallpaper.com/author/ellie-stathaki\" target=\"_self\">Ellie Stathaki, Wallpaper* architecture editor</a>,&nbsp;explore in detail the design approach, expression and impact of the commission. The book aims to&nbsp;open&nbsp;up a broader conversation about cities, pavilions, parks and public spaces today, and their role in creating healthier places to live and work.</p> \n<p>Reflecting on the book,&nbsp;Carme Pinós, founder of&nbsp;Estudio Carme Pinós said:&nbsp;‘Architecture is a service to society; it changes people’s lives and makes society possible. That’s why I liked Naomi Milgrom’s MPavilion project— it’s social, and it’s for the citizens to use. We need to find a new language and that time has come. It’s time for free and flexible architecture.’ §</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/uroBMUEAFxw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 18 Aug 2020 11:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/internals-mock-up-5.jpg","linkMd5":"440c8eb65cd84ab4c977d748539dbd78","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn63@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-15-053_7726a5e63dc40c7f.webp","destWidth":540,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":26650,"destBytes":23400,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/internals-mock-up-5.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/internals-mock-up-5.jpg?itok=3Q0TCp8b":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn76@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-20-077_e2b829288bb041ac.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/mockup.jpg?itok=SJ1hGKLK":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn94@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-20-202_d977ea5c78ce0eed.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/mp3.jpg?itok=X6MkGOVG":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn80@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-17-398_0f5d770a25349612.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/mp2.jpg?itok=x0EidZAc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn48@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-20-619_0d9cebeee953e594.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/uroBMUEAFxw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn4@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-17-433_b4bcb4fc7e3dd549.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696878},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"High school photography in focus at Harlem’s Studio Museum","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/63XWSzJ_ANk/expanding-the-walls-studio-museum-harlem","description":"<p>For the last 20 years, the Studio Museum in Harlem has nurtured the talents of a select group of New York high-school students with ‘Expanding the Walls’, an eight month photography-based programme that leads its participants to explore ideas of community, history and culture while learning technical basics.</p>\n<p>Working with a diverse group of artist professionals, and using the work of photographer James Van Der Zee as a jumping-off point, the students participate in art workshops, discussion groups and excursions before producing and presenting a collective body of work.</p>\n<p>It should be of no surprise that the end result of this year’s cohort has taken on a very different significance. When students could no longer gather with each other and their mentors, they had no option but to complete the programme remotely. This year’s exhibition, entitled ‘Hearts in Isolation: Expanding the Walls’, sees the programme’s 15 young creatives, who are aged between 15 and 18, capture glimpses of life in lockdown.</p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287324\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/april_riley_new-heights.jpg?itok=4Cyy-fK6\" width=\"1416\" height=\"944\" alt=\"\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\nApril Riley, <em>new heights</em>, 2020. Digital chromogenic print. <em>Courtesy of the artist</em>\n</div>\n<p>‘Community has always been a key pillar of the Expanding the Walls programme,’ says Thelma Golden, the museum’s director and chief curator. ‘This year, students and mentors faced an unprecedented set of new challenges that redefined that pillar. At a time when home can feel isolating, the programme created a critical space for community, and the camera became a tool for students to confront unexpected circumstances.’</p>\n<p>Ranging from portraits and self-portraits to still-life compositions and everyday scenes in the home, the poignant images on display in the online exhibition reflect a vivid array of perspectives, styles and issues, while exuding a level of introspection and artistry that truly goes beyond the artists’ years. §</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/63XWSzJ_ANk\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sun, 16 Aug 2020 08:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/april_riley_new-heights_feature.jpg","linkMd5":"09673563202183eabe0955536c767a65","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn91@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-15-685_2371b1cb53545d62.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":19989,"destBytes":16804,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/april_riley_new-heights_feature.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/april_riley_new-heights.jpg?itok=4Cyy-fK6":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn50@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-18-418_cc57973d2863f9a9.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/63XWSzJ_ANk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn23@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-20-391_6cdd8ee937427f4e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696879},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Artist’s Palate: Claudia Comte’s ‘Eggplant Baby’","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/AIARcD9fmiU/artists-palate-claudia-comte-eggplant-baby","description":"<p>Swiss artist Claudia Comte has had a meteoric rise in the past few years. From underwater cactus sculptures and dazzling Op Art interventions, to a forest of carved reliquaries and a functional fairground installed in Art Basel’s Messeplatz: her output is varied, memorable, and always characterised by a sense of humour. The same goes for her ‘Eggplant Baby’, a dish that she recently used to kick start the birth of her son, Kai Nikolai. The recipe originates from Scalini’s, an Italian restaurant in Georgia, US, and is said to have induced labour for more than 300 expectant mothers. As Comte recalls: ‘My partner, Samuel Leuenberger, prepared the meal for me, and remarkably within a few hours I was in labour!’ </p>\n<p><strong>Recipe for ‘Eggplant Baby’</strong></p>\n<p>‘The measurements below serve much much more than just a mother, baby, father family unit. It’s a dish that can be shared!’</p>\n<p><strong>Ingredients for the marinara sauce</strong></p>\n<p>2 teaspoons of grated garlic<br />\n3 teaspoons of olive oil<br />\n1.8kg chopped tomatoes (canned or fresh)<br />\n230g chopped onions<br />\n100g chopped parsley<br />\n1 teaspoon of oregano<br />\n1 teaspoon of grated red pepper<br />\n30g chopped basil<br />\n1 pinch of thyme<br />\n1 pinch of rosemary<br />\n1 teaspoon of salt<br />\n1 teaspoon of pepper</p>\n<p><strong>Preparation of marinara sauce</strong></p>\n<p>First, fry the onions in a large saucepan with hot olive oil. Add garlic and fry again for 1 minute. Now add the tomatoes and bring everything to the boil before you reduce the heat. Then put the remaining ingredients in the pot and let it simmer for 1 hour. Stir every now and then.</p>\n<p><strong>Ingredients for the eggplant and parmesan bake</strong></p>\n<p>3 medium sized eggplants<br />\n230g flour<br />\n6 beaten eggs<br />\n940g breadcrumbs of Italian bread (Ciabatta)<br />\n1.8 l marinara sauce<br />\n100g grated Romano cheese<br />\n100g grated parmesan cheese<br />\n100g chopped mozzarella cheese<br />\n460g ricotta cheese<br />\nOlive oil</p>\n<p><strong>Preparation for the eggplant and parmesan bake</strong></p>\n<p>Wash and chop the eggplants first (either with or without the skin is fine). Spread the eggplants on a paper towel and sprinkle them with salt. Put another layer of paper towel on top and weigh the whole thing down for an hour so that the excess liquid can flow out.</p>\n<p>Dip the individual pieces of eggplant one after the other in flour, beaten eggs and the breadcrumbs. You can then fry the eggplants in olive oil until they are golden brown. Spread the marinara sauce, eggplant, ricotta, parmesan and romano cheese alternately in layers in a baking dish until the baking dish is almost completely full. Then sprinkle a layer of mozzarella on top.</p>\n<p>Now everything goes into the oven for about 25 minutes at 190 degrees (please preheat).</p>\n<p>Bon appetit and good luck! §</p>\n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n<em>A version of this article originally featured in the September 2020 issue of Wallpaper* (W*257) – on newsstands now and available for free download <a href=\"https://www.wallpaper.com/design/september-2020-issue-free-download\">here</a></em><br />\n \n</div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/AIARcD9fmiU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 24 Aug 2020 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/untitled-2_feature.gif","linkMd5":"dd307c9f6e5d67f2ca075705f55e48fb","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn68@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-15-180_96fc07340878de3b.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":138157,"destBytes":14894,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/untitled-2_feature.gif","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/AIARcD9fmiU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn8@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-16-928_e844a344b25acf31.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696876},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"We’re adding these bucket bags to our summer style list","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/a-TsDMGeZdU/bucket-bags-women-ss20","description":"<p>You may have had an array of exciting activities doodled on your 2020 bucket list, most of which will now remained unticked. But in lieu of a list, may we present the benefits of a bucket bag to you instead. The shape of this wardrobe staple was a stalwart on the S/S 2020 catwalks – presented as a grown up, elegant option, somewhere between spring’s most miniature and XL styles.&nbsp;The benefit of the bucket bag lies in its voluminous shape, which allows you to practically carry all your essentials for modern life over your shoulder, including&nbsp;your hand sanitiser.</p> \n<p>We’ve looked to Loewe, Celine, Prada and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/chanel\" title=\"Chanel\">Chanel</a> for practical options in natural tones, that reflect our desire for timeless dressing.&nbsp;Loewe’s Balloon bag is a new addition to the Madrid label’s covetable accessories arsenal, featuring a smooth&nbsp;drawstring closure, and an alluringly inflated shape. Also weaving magic is Prada’s Tress bag, which features a bucket silhouette laced&nbsp;from soft leather. At Celine and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/chanel\" title=\"Chanel\">Chanel</a>, metal hardware brings a vintage-inspired touch to this hands-free style, suitable for those in search of chic, Left Bank-inspired dressing. Bucket list? With these covetable accessory&nbsp;options, you’ll forget you ever had one.&nbsp;§</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/a-TsDMGeZdU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 Aug 2020 10:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/celinef.jpg","linkMd5":"b98c1405118f76542242ecdce84afcb1","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn32@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-15-945_b9b73fc2d54e2c6e.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":29247,"destBytes":19272,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/celinef.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/a-TsDMGeZdU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn52@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-21-157_889dffd3d401332b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696878},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Four menswear designers celebrating the value of craft","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/_sl_ADH-t4A/four-menswear-designers-craft","description":"<p>After months of human interaction umpired by computer screen, pleasure now lurks in the raw edges and amorphic patches applied with meticulous care to a vintage workwear jacket. This artisanal aptitude isn’t new – silk and cotton garments have been mended and patched together since the 17th century in Japan and, in North West Nigeria, indigo dye pits have been in operation for over 500 years – but it is enjoying a resurgence in menswear.</p> \n<p>Many of the pre-industrial production methods employed by the four designers featured here are intrinsically sustainable and culturally profound. Evidence of the human hand takes on a more significant role at this unparalleled time: it forces us to reconsider our own place in the world. &nbsp;</p> \n<h2>Prospective Flow</h2> \n<p><img alt=\"Prospective Flow\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/flow.jpg\" /></p> \n<p>Prospective Flow was launched in Los Angeles in 2009 by three Japanese brothers with the founding motto: ‘Today is the future our ancestors dreamed of. Keep the heritage flowing.’ Daisuke, Yusuke and Kensuke Muramatsu are devoted to the concept of ‘Onko-chishin’, a Japanese expression meaning: the attempt to discover new ideas by studying the past through scrutiny of history. ‘There are a lot of things going on in the world right now – things are changing on a daily basis – and that’s why we need to maintain our concept even more,’ Daisuke says. ‘We still believe that the world is a better place today than it was in the past. The admiration of genuine products has been passed on from generation to generation.’ Their workwear-inspired clothes have a lived-in serenity. ‘Today, in the era of smartphones and social media, technology is evolving faster than ever. And although new technological advancements can be exciting, they can also become overwhelming. When it comes to garments that you are going to put next to your skin, people prefer the sense of warmth they can feel from something that’s made by hand.’</p> \n<div class=\"image-caption\"> \n <a href=\"https://www.prospectiveflow.com\" target=\"_blank\">prospectiveflow.com</a> \n</div> \n<h2>Adam Pogue x Smock</h2> \n<p><img alt=\"Pogue Adam Smock\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/pogue.jpg\" /></p> \n<div>\n ‘It’s become too easy to throw things out and replace them,’ the Los Angeles-based textile artist Adam Pogue says. He has created a line of limited edition French workwear jackets for Mohawk General Store’s in-house line Smock: ‘We came up with the idea to make back and elbow patches that I would sew onto the garments – essentially they were like tiny quilts. Each one unique. I’d cut into the jackets and repair them and the patchwork became more random.’ Each vintage piece is appliqued with hand-dyed fabrics by Pogue and in true artisanal fashion, each wears its patina with pride. ‘They’re great because of the oil stains and wear and tear they’ve collected over the years which kind of gives a roadmap for each jacket to repair and reuse.’ Pogue who studied architecture and \n <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/sculpture\" title=\"Sculpture\">sculpture</a>, first began crafting materials together to decorate his apartment, upholstering a sofa in Japanese boro-style. His serene constructivist collages champion a wider philosophy too: ‘If I can make something or repair something, (rather than buy something) then that’s what I’ll do.’\n <br /> &nbsp;\n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n <a href=\"https://www.mohawkgeneralstore.com/collections/smock-x-adam-pogue\" target=\"_blank\">mohawkgeneralstore.com/collections/smock-x-adam-pogue</a>\n</div> \n<div> \n <h2>Monad London</h2> \n <p><img alt=\"Monad London\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/monad2.jpg\" /></p> \n <div class=\"image-caption\"> \n  <em>Art Direction and photography: Ade Udoma and Michelle Helena Janssen</em> \n </div> \n <p>Much of our perception of textile craftsmanship is oriented around the ancient techniques of Japan with less attention paid to the Kofar Mata dye pits in Kano, North West Nigeria. ‘I grew up in Nigeria so I had an implicit awareness of African textile crafts but I didn’t appreciate it until recently,’ designer Daniel Olatunji says. Hearing about the indigo traditions of his homeland whilst a student at Central Saint Martins in London,&nbsp;Olatunji moved back after graduating and set about learning as much as he could. His clothes – made using antique linens and homespun cottons – reflect a new feeling in menswear that refuses to be rushed. ‘I enjoy the process of making. I find it very meditative. The first time I saw the Fulani tribesmen in the north of Nigeria that grow and hand-weave cotton I was completely awestruck. The looms are built by the artisans using materials found around them and recycling parts from previous looms. This is also a reaction to my time working for more corporate and commercial brands where you see so much waste,’ he says. ‘Reviving and maintaining these processes can go some way to creating a more sustainable model for our industry. And our planet.’</p> \n <div class=\"image-caption\"> \n  <a href=\"https://www.monadlondon.com\" target=\"_blank\">monadlondon.com</a> \n </div> \n</div> \n<h2>Koromo</h2> \n<p><img alt=\"Koromo Kyoto\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/koromo.jpg\" /></p> \n<p>With their opulent use of boro – the patchworking technique developed in the north of Japan during the Edo Period – the Kyoto-based label typifies how we often expect ‘crafted’ clothes to look. ‘Kyoto is a place where the making of clothes enters the field of fine arts,’ designer Kayoko Kondo says. ‘In our current world of advanced mechanisation, without the feeling of love, we can’t work tirelessly in same way as hundreds of years ago. What’s most important with clothing is first that you just love it and second, that you’ll live with it your whole life.’ When the label launched in 2004, Kyoto’s antique markets were stuffed full of elaborate antique kimonos, whilst old pieces of indigo-dyed cloth were thrown away. Now things are different: ‘Once I sewed some boro cloth together and made it into a jacket. I displayed it in my shop and many people gathered around, some frowning, some smiling, some criticising it loudly. It felt like being at one of the amusement parks I had gone to as a child!’ Kondo says. Now, there’s been a global shift to revisiting tradition: ‘Just as a wild stitch can be more attractive than a neatly aligned seam, the heart may throb more for rough nonconformity than for pristine fabric. Craftsmanship is a sensory realm.’&nbsp;§</p> \n<div class=\"image-caption\"> \n <a href=\"https://koromo-kyoto.com\" target=\"_blank\">koromo-kyoto.com</a>\n <br /> &nbsp; \n</div>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/_sl_ADH-t4A\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 17 Aug 2020 11:30:59 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/monadf.jpg","linkMd5":"d4c95349b55390a856a7b7dc433255a0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn55@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-15-455_1ae1bfc5d0ae46aa.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":16186,"destBytes":8852,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/monadf.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/flow.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn72@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-20-167_c76aedd98f3a8194.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/pogue.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn36@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-18-282_7ae061e5245547df.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/monad2.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn32@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-18-279_7b2540aad24b2825.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/koromo.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn56@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-21-303_1332051ac0352f91.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/_sl_ADH-t4A":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn56@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-18-423_1a3498bca516dcbb.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696879},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"In memoriam: Luchita Hurtado (1920 – 2020)","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/TtcagJ4N_gU/in-memoriam-luchita-hurtado-obituary-1920-2020","description":"<p>Luchita Hurtado passed away on 13 August at her home in Santa Monica, California. She would have celebrated her 100th birthday in October.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Born in 1920 in Maiquetía, Venezuela, Hurtado spent the last seven&nbsp;decades investigating notions of universality and transcendence through a playful and ever-evolving practice. Ranging from abstraction and mysticism to landscape and the body, her experimental work&nbsp;has always aligned with important moments of her life.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Even though she spent most of her career away from the spotlight, the artist initially started to engage with paint in her teenage years: after immigrating to New York in 1928, she studied <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/painting\" title=\"Painting\">painting</a> at the Art Students League. Her early work was abstract and colourful, mainly consisting of surrealist landscapes and tribal figures. At the time, Hurtado was associated with the geometric abstraction movement, and identified the canvas as a medium for intimate meditations on humanity, the cosmos, and nature.</p> \n<div class=\"image-caption\"> \n <div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n  <div id=\"file-287488\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n   <div class=\"content\"> \n    <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"6\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/15.hurta95655-hires.jpg?itok=7T7J6r4c\" width=\"1460\" height=\"897\" alt=\"\" />\n   </div> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p><em>Luchita Hurtado,&nbsp;Self Portrait,&nbsp;1973.&nbsp;© Luchita Hurtado.&nbsp;Courtesy the artist and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/hauser-wirth\" title=\"Hauser &amp; Wirth\">Hauser &amp; Wirth</a>.&nbsp;Photography: Jeff McLane</em> </p>\n</div> \n<p>In the early 1940s, Hurtado joined Dynaton, an international network of painters, writers, and intellectuals with whom she would regularly travel across the US and Mexico. The various styles she embraced spoke of her own experiences, as well as the places she visited: everywhere she went, the artist would draw on different values and movements, and adapt her practice accordingly.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Hurtado first presented her work in 1958, while taking part of a group show at the art centre in La Jolla in California. In the years that followed, she travelled regularly between Los Angeles and Taos, a small town in New Mexico. This peripatetic period saw a drastic shift in her creative process: highly influenced by the social movements that characterised the 1960s, she decided to leave behind abstraction to focus on producing naturalistic and figurative work. Among others, the artist developed a keen interest in the liberation of women, and gradually explored the female form in her work. This culminated in her series of contemplative self-portraits – the ‘I am’ paintings – in which she provides a personal and assertive perspective on femininity, while unveiling various aspects of sexuality through an intelligent selection of raw metaphors.&nbsp;</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287485\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"4\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/8.hurta94362-hires.jpg?itok=FbWeAaWC\" width=\"1416\" height=\"944\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287483\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/13.hurta95571-hires.jpg?itok=MF_E0I84\" width=\"1416\" height=\"944\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Above:&nbsp;Luchita Hurtado,\n <em>&nbsp;Untitled</em>,&nbsp;1969. Below:&nbsp;\n <em>Untitled,</em>&nbsp;c. 1970s&nbsp;\n <em>© Luchita Hurtado.&nbsp;Courtesy of the artist and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/hauser-wirth\" title=\"Hauser &amp; Wirth\">Hauser &amp; Wirth</a>.&nbsp;Photography: Jeff McLane</em> \n</div> \n<p>Following this series, Hurtado produced a range of surrealist landscapes wherein the human and face and body takes on the shape of natural elements such as mountains and sand dunes. These works emphasise the connection between the body and the natural environment, a recurring theme for the artist.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>The past few years were significant for Hurtado’s career: in 2018, her work was presented at the Hammer’s biennial exhibition, alongside installations by 31 other artists. A few months later, she was the subject of a solo retrospective at Serpentine Sackler Gallery in London, titled ‘I Live I Die I Will Be Reborn’, which articulated a fierce love of the planet and an urgent call to fight climate change. The exhibition travelled to LACMA in early 2020.</p> \n<p>‘We are close to committing suicide. This is not just a story, this is a matter of fact,’ Hurtado said of her environmentalism&nbsp;to the Financial Times last year . ‘There are far more species leaving than there are arriving, it’s all unbalanced. I don’t think I’d like it to be the end of the world, but there is a real danger. It’s up to us all.’ §</p> \n<div class=\"image-caption\"> \n <div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n  <div id=\"file-287486\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n   <div class=\"content\"> \n    <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"5\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/5.serpentine-galleries.jpg?itok=S1DnxuwK\" width=\"1380\" height=\"944\" alt=\"\" />\n   </div> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p>Installation view, ‘Luchita Hurtado: I Live I Die I Will Be Reborn’.&nbsp;Serpentine Galleries, London, England,&nbsp;2019.&nbsp;<em>© Luchita Hurtado.&nbsp;Photography: Hugo Glendinning</em> </p>\n</div>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/TtcagJ4N_gU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 15 Aug 2020 09:13:46 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/luchita-hurtado-feature.jpg","linkMd5":"cc20393ef222b3bb8ae38f94bbc2528b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn76@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-15-210_48e549471f9bb97a.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":40419,"destBytes":33832,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/luchita-hurtado-feature.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/15.hurta95655-hires.jpg?itok=7T7J6r4c":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn70@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-17-996_bcd6c362890f4e53.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/8.hurta94362-hires.jpg?itok=FbWeAaWC":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn44@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-17-652_a8a82d0d029db404.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/13.hurta95571-hires.jpg?itok=MF_E0I84":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn6@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-20-335_b31e01bbe1d5d912.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/5.serpentine-galleries.jpg?itok=S1DnxuwK":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn19@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-19-897_f902cb6095d38778.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/TtcagJ4N_gU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn60@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-30-863_3ca8788be4bc041c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696879},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Modern Rustic retreat in Georgia by architect Levan Mushkudiani ","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/LUGoo0nbIRg/rooms-hotel-kokhta","description":"<p>Amongst the spruce at the foot of Kokhta Gora, a 2,200 metre peak in a newly-developed resort just a hop and skip from Bakuriani, where Georgia’s Winter Olympians traditionally hone their skills, Rooms Hotel Kokhta is the latest addition to Temur Ugulava’s sleek design-oriented lifestyle chain.</p>\n<p>The ark-like wood and glass ski-in ski-out affair, conceived by architect Levan Mushkudiani sits at the confluence of ski runs, hiking and horseback trails, making it ideal for year-round use.</p>\n<p>For those who prefer indoor pursuits, expansive views from the health club and pool bring the outdoors in. Best described as Modern Rustic, interiors are the work of designers, Nata Janberidze and Keti Toloraia, who met whilst training at the Tbilisi Academy of Arts; warm, organic spaces, with a hint of place that make loving use of wood, stone and polished clay. §</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/LUGoo0nbIRg\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 22 Aug 2020 08:00:00 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/rooms_hotel_kokhta_1_0.jpg","linkMd5":"7ca80db5c83156d4acba452f0e21856e","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn68@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-14-728_45953c5d03a5e603.webp","destWidth":848,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":49339,"destBytes":35050,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/rooms_hotel_kokhta_1_0.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/LUGoo0nbIRg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn10@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-17-670_84f5ac1fc5d3ce66.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696877},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Beauty and destruction: Beirut’s design community struggles to rebuild in the aftermath of explosions","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/SNZNYtvoYkw/beirut-design-community-following-explosion","description":"<p>It was the end of a summer’s day in Beirut — the sparkling Mediterranean city once referred to as the ‘Paris of the Middle East’. While this time of year usually sees Beirutis and foreigners congregating in droves at the beach or having a festive dinner by the sea, the city was now in a two-week lockdown following a surge in Covid-19 cases. Most had just finished work and were inside their homes. The country was already reeling from a state of financial collapse, spikes in unemployment and even power shortages. But then something worse happened. Two massive explosions at the Port of Beirut resulted in a state of apocalyptic destruction that, at the time of publication, left around 220 dead, 400 still missing, 6,000 injured and 300,000 homeless in a matter of 24 hours. The blasts took place in a warehouse at the port after an estimated 2,750 tonnes of ammonium nitrate, which had been stored on site for six years, exploded into gigantic clouds of white and orange smoke.</p> \n<p>While the reason the explosive material ignited remains unclear, no sight can parallel the destruction and utter carnage of present-day Beirut. At least 8,000 buildings, mostly located in the historic quarters and pulsating cultural districts of Gemmayzeh and Mar Mikhael, were damaged; according to Unesco, around 60 historic buildings are at risk of collapse. Many of the conversations in this article were interrupted due to the bad connection after the explosion.</p> \n<p>[embed]https://www.instagram.com/p/CD4GuRmpF1U/[/embed]</p> \n<p>‘The blasts destroyed the cultural heart of Beirut,’ said designer <strong><a href=\"https://roulasalamoun.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Roula Salamoun</a></strong>, whose office in Hamra was fortunately not as damaged as those in the epicentre. ‘We are going through erasure, and that’s very dangerous because we already went through it after the civil war [of 1975–90]. If we wipe out <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/museums\" title=\"Museums\">museums</a> and institutions, then we wipe out our past and we wipe out our culture, our society and our heritage.’</p> \n<blockquote class=\"w-wide-quote\">\n <p> ‘The tragedy is that we are now just content at being alive – that’s how dramatic things have turned,’&nbsp;– Roula Salamoun </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>Beauty has always sat alongside violence in Beirut. It is no stranger to destruction and its urban landscape reflects its wounds. The civil war left the city severely scarred with bullet holes that permeated its historic structures for years. Then in 2006 another Israeli invasion left the city in a state of shock. Rebuilding has become a constant in the Lebanese mind and character. But even as Lebanese and designers courageously move to fix their beloved, many affirm that this time reconstruction must be different.</p> \n<p>Rania Abboud is the communication director of <strong><a href=\"https://pslab.net/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">PSLab</a></strong>, a design and manufacturing company of site-specific luxury <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/lighting\" title=\"Lighting\">lighting</a> products. Its Beirut headquarters is 500 metres away from the port. The blast left it in shambles, glass flew everywhere coupled with the blood of wounded staff.</p> \n<blockquote class=\"w-wide-quote\">\n <p> ‘One quarter of the city became white in the blast. It affected four of the most prominent streets in the city. Chaos was everywhere. I grew up during the war and I have never witnessed destruction like this,’&nbsp;– Rania Abboud, PSLab </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>‘The only building that we might be able to save is the <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/bauhaus\" title=\"Bauhaus\">Bauhaus</a> building on our premises. All the rest is gone.’, recalls Aboud.</p> \n<p>Located in Mar Mikhael, similarly near the port, is the studio and showroom of design duo <strong><a href=\"http://www.davidandnicolas.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">David/Nicolas</a></strong>, founded in 2011 by David Raffoul and Nicolas Moussallem. ‘For the first few days we were not even able to speak about what happened. It was so traumatic,’ says Raffoul, a week after the blast left their space in ruins.</p> \n<p>[embed]https://www.instagram.com/p/CDi7tNtnPj5/[/embed]</p> \n<p>Moussallem was just locking the door of the studio when the explosions happened. ‘The key broke in half,’ he remembers. He and a colleague were immediately blown through the glass and through the wall into the store next door. They quickly ran out of the shop, past the dead and the injured, covered in blood and trying to find refuge. ‘At first we thought there was a bomb in our space, but then we walked out and saw that the whole street was destroyed. We walked further and saw that the whole neighborhood was destroyed, and a bit more and we saw finally that the entire city was destroyed.’</p> \n<p>Raffoul was in the neighbourhood of Achrafieh, having just finished a gym session. ‘Nicolas called me and said, “David, there is no more studio,”’ he recalls.</p> \n<blockquote>\n <p> ‘We lost everything in one second. Material suddenly became not important when compared with our lives.’&nbsp; — David Raffoul, David/Nicolas </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>Raffoul rushed back to save their archive, their key to being able to rebuild. The building where their studio is located is a historic structure, and while it can be fixed, the risk of something falling on it and damaging it again is high. While Moussallem and Raffoul aim to repair it, they are presently planning to invest more in their Milan space.</p> \n<p>‘We are not able to fathom the damage,’ says designer <strong><a href=\"https://www.nadadebs.com/en/home\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Nada Debs</a></strong>, whose boutique and studio in Gemmayze were completely destroyed. ‘If you saw this in a movie, you would say it was too much and that it looked artificial, and that you would have to reduce the impact!’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287659\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rni-films-img-5f668a2c-4b72-424c-90e7-f766456962fa.jpg?itok=qCshRl3s\" width=\"750\" height=\"1000\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  A sketch of David/Nicolas’ ’Monocle’ cabinet lies among the debris of their destroyed studio in Mar Mikhael.\n <em> Image courtesy of David/Nicolas</em> \n</div> \n<p>Debs, like many architects and designers in Beirut, is eager to rebuild and stay in her beloved city, but not without a change. ‘All around us are these old beautiful homes with older people living in them, now completely destroyed, and that is heartbreaking,’ adds Debs, as she was walking to the protest that took place the Saturday after the explosion. ‘Most of the creative industry, all located in Gemmayze and Mar Mikhael, has been hit. Me and my team organised ourselves very quickly and now we are ready to help others. This is the way forward – we all need to support each other.’</p> \n<p>Located on Rue Verdun, one of the most prestigious streets in Beirut’s downtown, the flagship store of leading carpet purveyor <strong><a href=\"http://www.iwanmaktabi.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Iwan Maktabi</a></strong> was badly damaged. The brand’s antique carpets date back to the early 20th century in Isfahan, Iran, while their contemporary carpets feature designs from prominent brands including Jan Kath and CC-Tapis. There’s now a white piece of fabric hanging from the one of the windows, with English and Arabic writing declaring: ‘We are staying.’</p> \n<p>[embed]https://www.instagram.com/p/CDvUQq6Jhpq/[/embed]</p> \n<p>‘We have always been resilient enough to be able to keep rebuilding, but now I am offended when people call us Lebanese resilient,’ said Mohamed Maktabi, the company’s general manager. ‘The phoenix rising from the ashes — that’s what we always have been, but this time is different. I don’t want to build again — I have been building all of my life! I want to have the choice to die of natural causes and not like this!’&nbsp;But they, like many Beirutis, have vowed to rebuild, while knowing there is, as Maktabi says, ‘a high chance of it being destroyed again.’</p> \n<blockquote class=\"w-wide-quote\">\n <p> ‘The phoenix rising from the ashes — that’s what we always have been, but this time is different. I don’t want to build again — I have been building all of my life!’&nbsp;– Mohamed Maktabi, Iwan Maktabi </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>The destruction also reached the Al-Amin Mosque, a Sunni-Muslim mosque in downtown known for its blue dome and minarets, completed in 2008. Inside, the Ottoman-inspired decorations of <a href=\"http://www.dada-associates.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Nabil Dada</a>, one of the city’s most celebrated architects, were damaged. The walls were originally adorned with vibrantly coloured and patterned tiles, which Dada had spent two days sourcing in Iznik, Turkey, a town famous for its tile-making traditions that date back hundreds of years.</p> \n<p>Dada says the damage inflicted on the city actually could have been much worse. ‘If it weren’t for the grain silo building [built by Palestinian banker Yusuf Beidas in 1968, it took the brunt of the explosions’ impact], we would have lost even more,’ explains Dada on the phone from Beirut. ‘Buildings facing the port lost their entire façade. The colour of the cloud from the explosion was this orange that we had never seen before.’ Dada and his team are now in the process of cleaning the debris from their damaged office.</p> \n<p>[embed]https://www.instagram.com/p/CDvP2j1JLOd/[/embed]</p> \n<p>Even as architects and designers courageously work to reconstruct their homes and workspaces, many don’t have the means to rebuild as before. Lebanon’s financial collapse has left most in a state of constant uncertainty.</p> \n<p>‘I don’t have the means to rebuild my [showroom and] office like it used to be,’ says furniture and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/jewellery\" title=\"Jewellery\">jewellery</a> designer <strong><a href=\"https://www.karenchekerdjian.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Karen Chekerdjian</a></strong>. ‘I invested everything I have done over 15 years into it.’</p> \n<p>For the first ten days after the blast Chekerdjian decided to shutter her business. But then she changed her mind. ‘I can transform it into something else – a storage and a studio, but not a showroom anymore. I can’t shut it easily and need to find a way to fix it, but I don’t know how I will manage to rebuild because it’s very expensive.’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287658\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/karen_chekerdjian_02.jpg?itok=Sd3oQ-hh\" width=\"1460\" height=\"973\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Designer Karen Chekerdjian surveys the damage to her showroom, whose walls had been destroyed by the explosions of 4 August. The mobile still hanging in the space is the first piece of furniture she’d created. \n <em>Photography: Alain Sauma</em> \n</div> \n<p>She will continue to produce her <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/jewellery\" title=\"Jewellery\">jewellery</a> locally, but now focus entirely on international sales. ‘This is the reverse of what I have done before,’ she said. ‘My business used to rely on 80 per cent of sales from Lebanon and 20 from outside. I have to reverse it now, otherwise I cannot survive.’</p> \n<p>Entrepreneur and philanthropist Cherine Magrabi runs <strong><a href=\"https://www.houseoftoday.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">House of Today</a></strong>, a non-profit platform supporting emerging Lebanese designers. She was in France when the explosion hit, and quickly mobilised to help struggling Lebanese creatives.&nbsp;‘This explosion shattered their dreams, their future,’ she says. ‘We need to raise awareness locally and abroad.’</p> \n<p>House of Today is now helping designers to refurbish their studios, to get them back up and running as soon as possible. Those in need can fill out an application on its website. In the meantime, Magrabi is also raising money to provide opportunities for Lebanese students to pursue design studies abroad, and for designers to enroll in overseas residency programs.</p> \n<p>Even before the explosions rocked Beirut, the Lebanese economy was in a state of free fall. All sectors were affected. ‘The <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/jewellery\" title=\"Jewellery\">jewellery</a> industry was already at risk of disappearing,’ says <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/jewellery\" title=\"Jewellery\">jewellery</a> designer <strong><a href=\"http://www.gaellekhouri.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Gaelle Khouri</a></strong>. ‘Local demand for luxury products was already severely dampened in light of the capital controls imposed by Lebanese banks and the steep reduction in people’s purchasing power.’ Craftspeople, such as the Armenians who came to Lebanon for a better life, are now fleeing back to their home countries or to finding refuge elsewhere in the world, she notes.</p> \n<p>Khouri’s studio and apartment in Mar Mikhael are completely damaged. ‘The waiters in the coffee shop around the corner are both dead,’ she laments.</p> \n<blockquote>\n <p> ‘We are traumatised. This comes after a succession of crises that have hit Lebanon. The global community needs to help Lebanese creatives.’&nbsp;– Gaelle Khouri </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>The creative community, says Khouri, will rise again, just as Beirut will rise again. ‘As designers what we must do now is channel this trauma into our creativity,’ she tells me over the phone. To this end she has created a ring titled ‘I have a Dream’, reflective of Lebanon’s endurance over the last four decades. It is entirely handmade in Lebanon by local craftspeople impacted by the 4 August explosions, and has been donated &nbsp;to the Auverture Raffle for Beirut, to raise funds for the Lebanese Red Cross and Lebanese Food Bank. It shows a hand, mutilated yet clutching a blossoming flower. ‘It embodies the eventual re-birth of Lebanon, driven by the fortitude and resilience of its exceptional people,’ Khouri describes. ‘Lebanese citizens have always faced challenges head-on and learned to grow and adapt in the face of disturbing tragedies. Thanks to their strong aspiration for the beauty of life, Lebanon is today on its way to a revolution.’ §</p> \n<p>[embed]https://www.instagram.com/p/CD9V1UjFnKs/[/embed]</p> \n<p></p> \n<p>\n <div class=\"key-fact-module\">\n  <br /> \n  <h2>The people of Beirut are in urgent need of international support following the devastating explosions of 4 August. Please give generously to the following organisations if you can:</h2>\n  <br /> \n  <ul>\n   <br />\n   <strong>§&nbsp;&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.houseoftoday.com/Support-Us.aspx\">House of Today</a> </strong>and\n   <strong> <a href=\"https://slowfactory.foundation/superfund\">Super Fund</a> </strong>by environmental foundation Slow Factory are helping local designers restore their damaged studios\n   <br /> &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;\n   <br /> \n   <li><br /><strong>§&nbsp;&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.arabculturefund.org/News/102\">The Lebanon Solidarity Fund</a></strong> at the Arab Fund for Arts and Culture are supporting institutions and individuals in Beirut’s arts and culture sector<br /> </li>&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;\n   <br /> \n   <li><br /><strong>§&nbsp;&nbsp;<a href=\"https://supportlrc.app/\">The Lebanese Red Cross</a></strong> has launched a Beirut Emergency Appeal to save lives<br /> </li>\n   <br /> &nbsp;&nbsp;\n   <br /> \n   <li><strong>§&nbsp; <a href=\"https://www.justgiving.com/crowdfunding/lebanon-relief\">Impact Lebanon</a></strong> is distributing funds to local NGOs in priority sectors: healthcare, food, shelter<br /> </li>&nbsp;&nbsp;\n reconstruction, and social enterprises<br /> </li>&nbsp;&nbsp;\n   <br /> \n   <li><br /><strong>§&nbsp;&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.ulyp.org/donate\">Bebw’shebbek</a></strong>, a local initiative led by Mariana Wehbe and Nancy Gabriel, is helping to rebuild doors and windows of the 80,000 destroyed homes due to the explosions</li>\n  </ul>\n </div></p> \n<p>&nbsp;<br /> </p> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  &nbsp; \n</div>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/SNZNYtvoYkw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 21 Aug 2020 15:17:41 +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/beirut.jpg","linkMd5":"7fed1b1067a783840395ca3a52875641","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn40@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-14-825_c30723e156663942.webp","destWidth":484,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":56558,"destBytes":50310,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/08/beirut.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/rni-films-img-5f668a2c-4b72-424c-90e7-f766456962fa.jpg?itok=qCshRl3s":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn80@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-16-168_3b65a70079c90bb2.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/karen_chekerdjian_02.jpg?itok=Sd3oQ-hh":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn95@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-16-358_f06fe9a3a4629cfc.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/SNZNYtvoYkw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn27@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-17-142_57a14c4502e1b728.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696877},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Social distancing furniture: innovative solutions for post/pandemic design","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/YsrBoZo1FgE/social-distancing-furniture-innovative-pandemic-design","description":"<p>From subtle screens to adaptable furniture, here are some designers’ solutions to social distancing in the form of private, separating systems to ensure safe indoor spaces.</p> \n<h2>Integralis and Discovery Space by Artemide</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287507\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img alt=\"Socially distance furniture\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"9\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/discovery_space_01.jpg?itok=zwqSWO9w\" width=\"1460\" height=\"973\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>In the past few years, Artemide has been making great leaps in the scientific and technological aspects of <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/lighting\" title=\"Lighting\">lighting</a>, and their accumulated expertise make them particularly suited to cater to the current social distancing requirements. Fresh off the shelves is Integralis, an innovative light that helps sanitise surfaces by illuminating them – the UV light can act against pathogenic microorganisms in the environment and slow the growth of bacteria, fungi and mold. Built as an open platform, the technology can be applied to existing <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/lighting\" title=\"Lighting\">lighting</a> products, and activated via a specially-developed app. While it works combined with all of Artemide’s designs, it is particularly effective when paired with the Ernesto Gismondi-designed Discovery Space (pictured). A semi-transparent panel that can be installed vertically or horizontally, Discovery Space helps define the environment while offering a safe distancing solution.</p> \n<h2>COV by Matteo Cibic</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286624\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/matteocibic-divider-1a-black.jpg?itok=SZ55J1zf\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1460\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>Italian designer Matteo Cibic has conceived a range of partitioning glass screens, appropriately named ‘COV’, which play on the dichotomy of socialising in separation. Produced by FusinaLab, these delicate dividers are made from anodized aluminium and perspex and give a light and airy feel to environments such as office spaces, restaurants, bars or reception desks. The designs consist of subtle decorative details and come in various shapes and sizes including the fan-shaped ‘Flaneur’ model, all of which are defined by the joyful curves that are typical of Cibic’s works.</p> \n<h2>Hinoki by Federica Biasi</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286622\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/hinoki_screens_design_federica_biasi_for_manerba_5.jpg?itok=0pR1UfNW\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1039\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.wallpaper.com/handmade\" class=\"word-link\" target=\"_blank\">Wallpaper* Handmade</a> 2018 veteran, Federica Biasi, has designed a range of freestanding and tabletop panels called ‘Hinoki’ for Italian office furniture maker Manerba. These slim panels come in subtle tones that are based on the chromatic scheme of Manerba’s existing product line. Made from polycarbonate, which was chosen for its wearable, scratch-resistant and flexible qualities, the translucent panels physically divide spaces without compromising the feeling of spaciousness. Thanks to the semi-transparent material, the panels still allow for a sense of closeness to others nearby, as shapes and forms can be discerned through the vertical surface. The panel can be freestanding, on wheels or attached to a desktop. The modular system has endless customisable potential with the option of additional accessories such as cork, magnetic board, hooks, elastics, shelves and mobile hangers, while for spaces that require more privacy it can be covered in sound-absorbing fabrics.&nbsp;</p> \n<h2>L20 Sofa by JaK Studio</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286727\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"8\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/04_table_0.jpg?itok=NnxGSqzP\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1095\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>One of the biggest challenges in the face of the new work-from-home culture is the clash of our personal and work environments. Award winning JaK Studio have designed a solution for this by redesigning the classic L-shaped sofa to transform into a working pod. This versatile sofa can be folded out into a sofa bed with bedside table or turned on its side to create a sound proofed work space. Adapting to compact urban spaces, the L20 sofa concept looks at the most central feature in the home and how we can use this to transform the heart of the domestic space into a working environment. The idea behind the concept was inspired by JaK Studio’s director Jacob Low’s observation of his own children building dens during lockdown, using anything they could find around the home. ‘It dawned on me that the limit to what we can use our homes for is infinite if we are creative. The sofa bed was a revolutionary piece of furniture when it was first designed but it has not evolved further,’ says Low. Fitted with all the necessary work accessories including drop down desk, reading light, USB port and laptop charging points, this multipurpose piece of furniture gives us a real glimpse into future living.</p> \n<h2>Saltine and Toast by Pair</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286625\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"4\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/pair_saltine_12.jpg?itok=hHzt9eci\" width=\"1460\" height=\"1460\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>US-based Pair has devised two models of unobtrusive dividing screens for office spaces. The studio is specialized in functional furniture specifically for the workspace, and these two new projects expand on their expertise. Saltine (pictured here) is a clever solution that attaches a screen on brackets to existing office desks, eliminating the concern of these structures taking up valuable floor space. The adjustable height provides the user with the option of a completely enclosed private space or a more informal desk division. Made from materials that can be easily cleaned such as glass and acrylic, Pair has created a universal solution for a sanitised and focused desk space that reduces the spread of germs in compact working environments.</p> \n<p>Toast is an adaptable, floorstanding space divider&nbsp;made from lightweight material and allows for&nbsp;work separation wherever needed, not least because it can be turned horizontally or vertically depending on the height required. This sound-absorbing panel comes in a variety of playful or subtle colours and tones. Made from recycled materials, it creates a quiet, private environment whilst also sparking collaborative innovation as its tackable surface is the perfect moodboard.&nbsp;</p> \n<h2>Screens by Tihany Design</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286626\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"5\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/_amber_screen_view.jpg?itok=6Bjqa7eI\" width=\"1460\" height=\"973\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>Experts in hospitality design, Tihany’s new solution to dining diligently in these unprecedented times is a series of elegant concepts&nbsp;featuring screens made of glass, and wooden decorative panels for his hospitality clients. These easily moveable and cleaned screens can be adapted in their design details to fit seamlessly with the interiors of the restaurant; so much so, that once in place they feel as if they have always been there.</p> \n<p>Providing a more intimate dining experience, the varying designs feature sleek geometric shapes evocative of the Art Deco style. ‘The screens are designed with wellness and comfort in mind, but they actually evoke a kind of public privacy that we feel might actually offer a positive spin on rebuilding the experience of dining out,’ says Tihany’s Managing Partner Alessia Genova. ‘It becomes an opportunity to focus on the faces and food in front of you while still enjoying the atmosphere, the service, and everything we’ve been missing while apart from our favorite restaurants’. These screens will help restaurants from Miami’s Novikov to Hong Kong’s Amber (pictured) as they navigate reopening safely and adapt to the new normal for dining out.</p> \n<h2>Clikclax by Zahava</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286627\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"6\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/gum_03.jpg?itok=PgDz8U1D\" width=\"1460\" height=\"973\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>Australian designer Zahava Elenberg has conceived a fun and uplifting desk tidy system which provides the necessary divisions to ensure a clean and safe working environment. Clikclax is a bright multicoloured and multi-purpose system of interlocking perspex sheets which nods to the vintage children’s game, Playplax, synonymous with toys in the 70s. Comprising varying shapes and sizes of sheets that interlock with the cylindrical bases to form shelves, divisions and storage solutions, the desktop system has endless functionality and adaptability to be erected on any flat surface.</p> \n<p>‘Clikclax is a verb as well as a noun,’ Zahava says. ‘My dream is to see people Clikclaxing all over the world — in a range of situations, from offices and co-working spaces to schools and student accommodation, hotels, galleries and libraries, at communal tables in cafes and restaurants, and on floors for kids to play together, apart. I can also see Clikclax working on a larger scale, as people-sized partitions and wayfinding demarcations. There’s nothing we can’t click together.’</p> \n<h2>Dutch Invertuals</h2> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-286677\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"7\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/dutch_invertuals.jpg?itok=4X6xvghv\" width=\"1460\" height=\"973\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<p>When it comes to room dividers, this Eindhoven-based design collective is not trying to blend in. Their custom-made dividers available in four varying shapes (circular, oval, diamond and crescent) ensure safe surroundings are achieved with a light and uplifting aesthetic. Distinctive in their bold and bright material options of acrylic transparent, acrylic translucent and fabric, they can also be customised in shape, size and colour combinations upon request. The dividers, which are made in the US, can be combined and layered to create life sized, vertical geometric montages. Mounted on a powder coated steel frame on wheels, they are easily moveable and bring life to anywhere indoors in need of a safe and sanitised solution.§</p>\n<img +0000","feedId":9788,"bgimg":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/07/matteocibicdivider-fusinalab-1a.jpg","linkMd5":"345809e9e201c823f9aa942f6cd54753","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn71@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-35-15-181_9868e988c152f054.webp","destWidth":565,"destHeight":565,"sourceBytes":35693,"destBytes":25280,"author":"","enclosureType":"image/jpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/wp_large/s3/2020/07/matteocibicdivider-fusinalab-1a.jpg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/discovery_space_01.jpg?itok=zwqSWO9w":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn12@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-35-19-753_8835bdf7cbd39f61.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/matteocibic-divider-1a-black.jpg?itok=SZ55J1zf":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn46@2020_1/2020/08/25/00-35-21-195_f971067d199d94ed.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/hinoki_screens_design_federica_biasi_for_manerba_5.jpg?itok=0pR1UfNW":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn74@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-18-740_1344c3ad4beaa86a.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/04_table_0.jpg?itok=NnxGSqzP":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn76@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-19-553_de49074ce0eca794.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/pair_saltine_12.jpg?itok=hHzt9eci":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn13@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-35-18-316_aaca04ef292bff36.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/_amber_screen_view.jpg?itok=6Bjqa7eI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn86@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-21-464_9e4d9db603059356.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/gum_03.jpg?itok=PgDz8U1D":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn89@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-35-20-143_a14eba13d93e0a14.webp","https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/dutch_invertuals.jpg?itok=4X6xvghv":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn19@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-17-115_24fed478b7b6651b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~4/YsrBoZo1FgE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn66@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-35-18-580_c50f0a73d2fa31db.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598315696880},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:34:56","title":"Annie Leibovitz on the legacy of Henri Cartier-Bresson","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/HHAT81fcOXI/annie-leibovitz-henri-cartier-bresson-palazzo-grassi","description":"<p>Leibovitz remembers, as a young <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/painting\" title=\"Painting\">painting</a> student at the San Francisco Art Institute, studying one of the iconic photojournalist Henri Cartier-Bresson’s most famous images.&nbsp;The now illustrious American portrait photographer found the image in the university’s library, in a newly published book titled <em>The World of Henri Cartier-Bresson.</em></p> \n<p>A member of the French army during the Second World War, Cartier-Bresson escaped from a prisoner-of-war camp in 1943 and joined a French underground photographic unit, covering the liberation of France and the retreat of the German army. In the spring of 1945, he found himself photographing a displaced persons camp in Dessau, Germany, between the American and Soviet zones.&nbsp;‘The photograph shows the moment when a Gestapo informant is confronted by a woman she betrayed,’ Leibovitz writes. ‘The informant is humiliated, the betrayed woman in a rage and about to strike a blow, the spectators in thrall at the scene – all conveyed with great simplicity.’</p> \n<p>In the catalogue of a new exhibition ‘Le Grand Jeu’, on show now at Palazzo Grassi, Venice, Leibovitz says: ‘Seeing Cartier-Bresson’s work made me want to become a photographer. The idea that a photographer could travel with a camera to different places, see how other people lived, make <em>looking</em> a mission — that that could be your life was an amazing, thrilling idea.’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287305\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/004_hcb.jpg?itok=196rlNNh\" width=\"1398\" height=\"944\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Henri Cartier-Bresson, \n <em>Dimanche sur les bords de Seine, France, 1938</em>, gelatin silver print, 1973.&nbsp;\n <em>© Fondation Henri Cartier-Bresson / Magnum Photos.</em>&nbsp;Selected by Annie Leibovitz as part of her&nbsp;curated section of&nbsp;‘Le Grand Jeu’ at Palazzo Grassi \n</div> \n<p>The exhibition’s title ‘Le Grand Jeu’ is a reference to the group of photographs that Cartier-Bresson, the man now best known for coining the term ‘the decisive moment’, considered to be his ‘Master Collection’. The selection was made in 1972, in Houston, in the home of the renowned photography collectors John and Dominique de Menil.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>When Cartier-Bresson stayed with them one summer, they asked the French photographer to compile his selection. Almost 50 years later, the 385 photographs he chose for the de Menils have been gathered at Palazzo Grassi, in an exhibition born of a partnership between the Bibliothèque nationale de France in Paris, the Pinault Collection and the Fondation Henri Cartier-Bresson.But, as a way of providing an additional element of curation to Cartier-Bresson’s selection, the exhibition’s overall curator,</p> \n<p>Matthieu Humery, invited five co-curators to provide their response to Cartier-Bresson’s work.&nbsp;Annie Leibovitz was one of those curators, alongside art historian Sylvie Aubenas, writer Javier Cercas, collector François Pinault and film director Wim Wenders.&nbsp;Each had to follow a basic rule; they were to choose 50 images from amongst the 385. Beyond that, they were given free reign to do whatever they liked with the sequencing and editing of Cartier-Bresson’s work.</p> \n<blockquote>\n <p> ‘You can see the way Cartier-Bresson composed. How he found a situation that he liked and then waited for something to happen within it’ </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>To contend with the task, Leibovitz first pinned all the photographs, which she had printed the size of index cards, in rows on a wall of her studio. She then picked out the pictures that had a strong influence on her work and remained etched on her mind from her time as a student.&nbsp;‘Some of the photographs I chose are hard to look at. Some of them are important, famous pictures because of their subjects,’ Leibovitz says.</p> \n<p>‘You can see the way Cartier-Bresson composed. How he found a situation that he liked and then waited for something to happen within it. How there is something going on in the foreground and something else in the background or in another part of the scene. It’s almost the way your eye sees. Something is happening in front of you, and something else is happening farther off. It’s interesting to see what he does over and over again.’</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287306\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/hcb_install_1.jpg?itok=BIQrEo5L\" width=\"944\" height=\"1416\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Installation view of&nbsp;Annie Leibovitz’s curated section in ‘Le Grand Jeu’&nbsp;at&nbsp;Palazzo Grassi, Venice \n</div> \n<p>Leibovitz met Cartier-Bresson once, in 1976, when she was a young photographer at <em>Rolling Stone</em>. Jann Wenner, the magazine’s editor, asked her to work on a special issue of the magazine, in which she would make a series of portraits of other photographers. Included were Andy Warhol, Richard Avedon, Jacques-Henri Lartigue, Helmut Newton and Ansel Adams.</p> \n<p>Leibovitz wrote a letter to Cartier-Bresson at his home in Paris and got no response. ‘So I made a pilgrimage to Paris to find him. I didn’t have an appointment. I just went to the Magnum office and he happened to be there,’ she says.&nbsp;Leibovitz walked home with Cartier-Bresson before having lunch at his house. ‘But he continued to refuse to let me photograph him or interview him,’ she says.&nbsp;</p> \n<blockquote>\n <p> ‘I made a pilgrimage to Paris to find him. I didn’t have an appointment. I just went to the Magnum office and he happened to be there.&nbsp;But he continued to refuse to let me photograph him or interview him’&nbsp; </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p>Leibovitz left without a portrait, and had a sleepless night. Early the next morning, she went back to a footbridge they had walked over the day before. ‘It was on his way to Magnum, and I thought that maybe he would come there again,’ she says. ‘It was neutral territory.’&nbsp;After an hour, Cartier-Bresson appeared on the street, and Leibovitz started taking pictures without him being aware of her presence.</p> \n<p>‘When he realised that the person taking photographs was me, he started yelling,’ Leibovitz remembers. ‘I didn’t understand everything he said, because I was concentrating on the pictures, but the gist was that I had betrayed him,’ Leibovitz says.</p> \n<p>‘He composed himself, and we walked together to the Magnum office. Along the way he explained that he didn’t want to be photographed because he felt that he wouldn’t be able to do his work on the street if people knew what he looked like,’ Leibovitz says. ‘They would behave differently if they knew he was taking their picture. This is something I only understand now.’ §</p> \n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\"> \n <div id=\"file-287307\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"content\"> \n   <img class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/003_hcb.jpg?itok=FinzJK2W\" width=\"944\" height=\"1399\" alt=\"\" />\n  </div> \n </div> \n</div> \n<div class=\"image-caption\">\n  Henri Cartier-Bresson,&nbsp;\n <em>Bougival, France</em>, \n <em>1956</em>, gelatin silver print, 1973 \n R.C.TECH","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/Wallpaperfeed/~3/a2pn4ZQ1DDw/neratza-beach-house-rctech-greece","description":"<p>On a flat piece of land along the coastline of the Northern Peloponnese region in Greece, overlooking the Parnassos mountain to the north, a single level holiday house stretches out along the coastline, with a beach-side swimming pool running parallel to the sea. Designed by R.C.TECH, the holiday house was designed for a young family in love with the Greek coastal landscape.</p>\n<p>The peaks and angles of the white pitched volume and the exposed concrete pergola create a moment of interest in the largely flat landscape, where the sea and the horizon are the only elements of context. The shape of the house is a product of the interior and exterior rooms that interlock to create a diverse number of spaces. The architects see each unique room in the house as a device for making holiday memories.</p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287697\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Beach house in Greece\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"1\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/neratza_house-24_0.jpg?itok=xwTuisGN\" width=\"1460\" height=\"821\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287698\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Aerial view\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"2\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/neratza_house-66cr_0.jpg?itok=o4AJ7JDo\" width=\"1460\" height=\"563\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The architecture balances a coastal lifestyle – where people gather together for late breakfasts at midday and games of cards in the evenings, with quiet and private moments for reading and thinking. While each space has been carefully thought out, the plan is remarkably simple. Two wings for sleeping extend out either side of the central yellow front door. At the heart of the plan is the living space – where kitchen, living and dining are softly separately by the open fireplace, and the two large cylindrical skylights that bring daylight into the kitchen and dining spaces.</p>\n<div class=\"media-element-container media-wysiwyg\">\n<div id=\"file-287699\" class=\"file file-image file-image-jpeg\">\n<div class=\"content\">\n    <img alt=\"Yellow door\" class=\"media-element file-wysiwyg\" data-delta=\"3\" src=\"https://cdn.wallpaper.com/main/styles/responsive_1460w_scale/s3/neratza_house_0.jpg?itok=-tpM3i4w\" width=\"1460\" height=\"975\" /></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>An extension of the interior, the outdoor living space sites between pool and indoor living room. Defined by the concrete frame of the pergola, the terrace is orientated towards the beach and the sea, while to south a small courtyard forms a quiet sanctuary.</p>\n<p>For R.C.TECH, designing a beach house is serious business – a holiday home is a very special place, where family memories are made and identities are formed. The yellow door for example was designed as a memorable feature for children, while the interplay of shadows and sunlight created to encourage contemplation in time, nature and the earth. §</p><img accelerated – and it is this heightened sense of the virtual, combined with a relative withdrawal from the external world, that has emphasised our engagement with our own physical products, spaces and environments. And our two covers have fully embraced this.</p> \n<p>For her newsstand cover and 22-page feature, conceptual artist Clare Strand was inspired by Edwin Abbott Abbott’s <em>Flatland: A Romance of Many Dimensions</em> to create a series of graphic interventions on George Harvey’s black-and-white fashion photography. She was drawn to the idea of readers engaging and interacting with the images and even creating their own artworks. Much like the protagonist of <em>Flatland</em>, we’re being challenged by Strand to seek a new perspective, cutting and folding to create three-dimensional objects from the two-dimensional templates presented on the pages. The monochrome palette accentuated Strand’s geometric forms, with the artist explaining ‘it helps boil the world down its essentials’.</p> \n<p>Virgil Abloh contributes our limited-edition cover to coincide with his collaboration with Mercedes-Benz. He was likewise drawn to the idea of cutting as activation, creating a dotted-line-with-scissors graphic to encourage readers to cut the magazine in half. It’s a bold gesture that celebrates the tactility and sheer actuality of print. Reinforcing engagement with the physical object, Abloh explained he wants people’s fingerprints to be left on the matte white surface. He will be personally cutting a special series of 100 copies, videoing the process (see&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/wallpapermag/?hl=en\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">@wallpapermag</a>) and selling these on <a href=\"http://canary---yellow.com\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">canary---yellow.com</a>, with all proceeds going to the Virgil AblohTM “Post Modern” Scholarship Fund to benefit Black students of academic promise.</p> \n<p>September Wallpaper* is our Style Special and reflects the seemingly effortless talent of our fashion director Jason Hughes. With ideas and vision aplenty, he has injected such a modern, fresh take on the season. He’s also mastered new ways of working in the era of social distancing, styling via Zoom and calmly taking challenges in his stride.</p> \n<p>Harriet Quick tackles this changing fashion landscape in her piece, ‘The Show Must Go On’, surveying the industry’s innovative solutions to limitations on travel and public gatherings. While producing this issue, we witnessed the birth of shows-in-a- box – notably with Loewe and its exquisite and collectable M/M Paris-designed cabinet of curiosities, and <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/bottega-veneta\" title=\"Bottega Veneta\">Bottega Veneta</a>’s Tyrone Lebon film, which came in a green neon box with projector included. They’re finding ways to still be physical, to engage, to touch. We’re craving this now and brands are responding. But it’s the co-existence that is most exciting, how far we can push the digital experience and make the physical more desirable, more intimate.</p> \n<p>Elsewhere, we celebrate Armani Casa’s 20th anniversary with a peek into the home of a pair of imaginary clients; Tilly Macalister-Smith talks to <a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/tags/david-adjaye\" title=\"David Adjaye\">David Adjaye</a> and Samuel Ross in a wide-ranging conversation touching on architecture, fashion, anti-racism and the future of the creative industries in a post-Covid world; artist Silvia Rosi delivers the season’s nest womenswear in a series of self-portraits; and Jaeger-LeCoultre peels back the curtain on the rare craft of guilloché.
